equally brave, stood at the top of the i

During the fighting on the north side

MASSACRE
OF WHITES
BY YAQUIS
Mexican Indians Slaying
All the Settlers They :
j^r Come 7 Across.
THOUSANDS^ ARMS
*' ,' ' â¦/' â:-"-y-.- >'
General' Torres \ Mobilizing Troops
for a War of Extermination
y â  Against the Reds.;
i
! â
Special Dispatch to The Call. . ' ,
ORTIZ, July 30.âAny doubt that the
Yaquis are on the â¢ warpath in deadly
earnest was dispelled when a courier
arrived to-day with news of the killing
of several Americans and Mexicans at
points In the central and lower Yaqui
valleys. The courier says the Indians
are waging a bloody war upon all set
tlers and that he has positive evidence,
of the slaughter. J. F. Remley. a mer
chant of Hermosillo, and E. Miller, a
photographer in his employ, both
Americans who were traveling through
the small settlements in the valley, are
among the killed.
The victims were surprised and shot
down without quarter. The entire coun
tryside is in terror and people are flee
ing from pueblos and mining camps to
the settlements. It is estimated that
from 2000 to 3000 Indians are in arms.
This does not represent their total
fighting strength, however, for several
thousand are working in the larger
towns, and these send their earnings to
the braves in the field and thus furnish
sinews erf -war.'
The outbreak is a surprise to General
Torres, who subdued the' Yaquis in
May, 1887, after a hard campaign of
more than two years. He was at the
Yaqui capital, Torin, only a few weeks
ago and discovered no indications that
the hatchet was being dug up. The
general is now in the field with the
Twelfth regiment, and other troops are
being mobilized as rapidly as possible.
Colonel Kosterlitzky, formerly of the
United States army, is gathering troops
at Magdalena. 220 miles from the scene
of trouble, and will reinforce General
Torres. A battle is expected in a few
days. ;'.}Â»-* 'â ?'
It is impossible to estimate as yet the
number of whites killed, inasmuch as
the- Indians are devastating a wide
stretch of country. .
At the signing of the treaty of Tin
two and a half years ago General
Torres told the. Yaquis that another
outbreak would mean a war of exter
mination. Torres was in earnest and a
bloody reckoning may be expected.
FROM TRANSPORT TO CAMP
THROUGH CHEERING CROWDS
Royal Welcome for the Men From Utah and Nebraska
Returning From Manila.
Cheers and Flags and Tooting Whistles Mark the Progress of the
7 ' Volunteers to Their Last Military Home at
the Presidio.
f --jHERK were more flags, more
7 "; cheers, more whistles and more
- enthusiasm yesterday as the
1 Nebraska men and the Utah
men marched up Market street
on their way to the Presidio. There
were thousands of welcomers on the
wharf and 'there were thousands more
along the "line of the parade. There
was a whistle going wherever there
was a factory or an engine of any
kind. and _ the steam blown away in
adding to tire din would carry the sol
diers back again to Manila, did they
wish to go.
The troop? left the transport at â¢ 9
o'clock and?"- formed on the- dock,- the
regiment in front and the two batteries
of artillery in the rear. The escort
sent from the Presidio formed on East
street and waited for the word. When
it came .-arid the newcomers marched
out of the dock those around the en
trance gave a ringing cheer and it was
taken up and whirled along the. water
front by the lines of watchers. until the
steamboats and factories joined in the
chorus to swell the boisterous greeting.
Down East street to Market, out Mar
ket to Golden Gate avenue then to Van
Ness and then out Lombard to the
camp the men passed through almost
unbroken lines of people, and every one
was cheering and every second one had
a flag which he waved vigorously upon
the least occasion. The ladies of the
Red Cross Society were at the wharf,
and they gave each man a bunch of
flowers to decorate rifle or hat or
blouse or haversack.
The battle flags were there, too, and
their progress up the street could be
followed by the renewed outbursts from
the crowds as they caught sight of their
tattered folds. The regimental flag of
the Nebraskans is little more than' a
shred and their national colors are
worse; the guidons of the Utah bat
teries are torn to ribbons, and the col
ors in all of them are faded and dim.
But the men are 'very proud of them
and they will hold the place of honor
among the treasures of the States from
which they came.
The mascot of the Utah batteries
marched with his comrades, proudly
bearing the silver cup the batterymen
had won on the baseball field at Ma
nila. There was a tournament held by
teams from six of the regiments and
the Utah men won the trophy. The
mascot is Robert Donohue. a lad of
twelve years of age, who has been With
the batteries since they left home a
year ago. "He was in three battles with
the troops, one at .San Fernando and
two at the waterworks, but he came
out of them all unscathed. .../ ;^i
The part of the parade that created
the greatest sensation was the line of |
ambulances carrying the men who
were not able to stand the long walk
from the wharf to the Presidio. There
Were ten of them, all filled with pale,
emaciated soldiers, some with crutches
beside them, some with heads band
aged, some with their arms in slings
and all showing plainly in look and at
titude what the island war is costing.
The sick men were cheered to the echo,
and they livened up and cheered back.
Men shouted words of encouragement
to them and women broke past the po
lice and threw* flowers into the ambu
lances, and all along the line the course
of the hospital wagons was. one of tri
umph.
[ At the corner of Van Ness avenue
and Bush street the troops were re
viewed by General Shafter and Gover
nor Poynter of Nebraska, ' and then
after reaching the Presidio they were
drawn up in line on the inner parade
ground and the Governor addressed
them briefly and gave them greeting.
He referred to their brilliant work and
their uncomplaining obedience and he
ended by' declaring that Nebraska was
proud of her sons.
The troops were reviewed once more
Governor Poynter, Adjutant General
.Barry of Nebraska and Adjutant Gen
eral Charles S. Burton of Utah. The
Utah men lined up with the Nebraska
men" and marched past the reviewing
officers with them. -v7^ ;-.V" :
The men .were then marched at once
out to the camp where as quickly as
'possible. they were assigned to quarters
and the work of preparing dinner was
commenced. They marched up from i
the transport in heavy marching order
with this exceptionâ the Utah men had
no arms. They left their guns and re
volvers In the Philippines with the men
of the Third Artillery, and they car
ried only their blankets and haver
sacks. Each man had one meal in his
haversack to guard against letting the
men go hungry should anything delay
the commissary department, but there
was no delay except from the late ar
rival of the troops and before 1 o'clock
the mess call was sounding merrily
down the company streets.
Ths Nebraska Regiment has been as
signed to the tents adjoining those of
the Oregon Regiment and the Utah
men have been put on the hill over
looking the camp on the old parade
ground. They were not long in settling
; themselves in their new quarters, and
mâ tea tea lea tea fen te] fcÂ» >â __â  SB MB Js_3 6__k
by the middle of the afternoon the camp
was in fairly good running order, lines
of men were coming and going to and
from the straw pile and there were few
who did not sleep last night upon a
well-filled mattress.
So far no programme for the govern
ment of the troops has been outlined,
but it is certain that they will be given
the fullest liberty. There was a small
guard set yesterday afternoon, but it
had not much in the way of duty to
perform. To-day the men will be told
what they can do and what is forbid
den.
The work of mustering out the men
from the Hancock will take three weeks
anyway. The Utah batteries may be
finished inside of two weeks, for on the
way over Major Grant had his officers
prepare most, of the necessary papers
and they are ready for the mustering
officer now. Besides there will be very
little in the way of ; a property settle
ment in the case of the batteries, for
they left most of their property with
the military establishment in Manila.
Every one of the men is loud in his
praise of the way in \ which they have
been received and loudest of them all
is Governor Poynter. He is going back
to Nebraska probably this evening and
he declares he will make a most flatter
ing report of the .homecoming of the
regiment when he gets there. "I will
say to the people of Nebraska," he said
yesterday, "that the men could not
have been more heartily welcomed had
they belonged to this city instead of
PRICE FIVE CENTS.
back in Nebraska. It was the greatest
welcome I have ever seen and we are
all grateful. I will say at home that
the regiment is well and that the men
are bearing modestly the fame they
have won. As for myself, I have none
but the kindest thoughts of San Fran
cisco. I have been shown every cour
tesy by the city officials, by the army
officers and by the business men. I
have been given every assistance I
could need and I shall always be grate
ful for the way in which our beloved
regiment and myself have been received
by the people here."
Adjutant General Burton of Utah ar
rived Sunday evening. He came to be
on hand when the batteries landed and
to consult with Major Grant on how to
get the men home. "We will not let the
batteries be scattered," said he yester
day. "Our boys have done too well to
allow them to break away from each
other now. We will bring them home
together and we will give them a recep
tion at Salt Lake that they will long
remember."
All the men who came in Saturday
night have been provided with warm
clothing. The Nebraska men. in addi
tion to heavy underclothes which they
drew while on their way here, will be
furnished with a chest protector apiece,
the chest protectors having been sent
out from home by their own State.
The Utah men have also drawn heavy
clothing, so there will be no increase of
the sick list from cold or exposure.
Every tent has a stove and there is
plenty of coal, every men has a good
blanket and a straw mattress can be
had for the filling of it: there is plenty
to eat and there is nothing to do but
rest, so the life of the troops from now
until they start East should have but
the one careâ to see their native States
The Nebraska boys owned the Orph
eum last evening and will continue in
control to-night and to-morrow night.
Owing to the exertions of the citizens'
executive committee the entire regi
ment will see the splendid entertain
ment provided at this popular house.
Yesterday afternoon a representative
of the citizen?' executive committee
i and a representative of the Orpheum
went out to the Presidio and presented
Colonel Mulford with 469 tickets for
last night's performance. Of these
nineteen were for the officers and 350
were distributed among the men of the
regiment and 100 among the members
of the Utah Battery. This afternoon
i 454 tickets will be distributed among
the soldiers and to-morrow night all
who have not seen the performance will,
be made welcome.
The soldiers thoroughly enjoyed the
programme last- night and there were
so many encores that it was late before
the last number was given. A section
in the center of the house had been re
served for the men and when a num
ber pleased them a thunder of applause
went up from that quarter that set
everybody else applauding. After their
year's service in Manila a night in the
Orpheum seemed like fairyland to the
war-worn soldier boys. After the en
tertainment they returned to camp sat
isfied that they are really and truly
home again and that there are some
I pleasures left in life after all.

NO ABATEMENT OF
YELLOW FEVER AT
HAMPTON HOME
Disease Probably Brought by a
Santiago Veteran- General
Quarantine in Force.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, July 31.â The latest I
official advices received up to 8 'dock i
to-night show a total of thirty-seven
cases and seven deaths from yellow
fever at the Soldiers' Home at Hamp
, ton, Va. One new case developed at
the home day. Thus far the disease
has been confined to the home, but all â 
surrounding towns are excited and a j
vigorous quarantine is being main- 'â 
tamed. Dr. Wadin of the Marine Hos- j
pital Service is now in charge of af- j
fairs at Hampton and is working in- â 
co-operation with the local Boards of
Health. He has strengthened the cor
don about the town of Phoebus, which ,
he reports is in very bad sanitary con- |
dition. The fact that only one new case j
appeared to-day is encouraging to the \
officials here, who are taking even- j
measure to prevent .1 spread of the :
disease. They feel that the fact that
the affected locality is in the hands of
the Government and under the one |
management will be of great value In
dealing with the scourge and will en- |
able better results to be obtained than j
where the epidemic broke out in com- j
mercial places. Already a number of |
inquiries have been received by Dr.
Wyman from quarantine officers j
througout the United States, asking
whether they shall Impose a maritime
quarantine against Norfolk. He has J
replied that there appears to be no ne- |
cessity for such a restriction in the
case of either Norfolk or Newport
News.
Dr. Wadin's official report of his ex
amination of the existing conditions
at the Soldiers' Home reached Dr. Wy
man to-day. It is dated yesterday and j
notes the fact that the doctor has seen j
all the cases of suspected disease at
the home. He sums up the history of
the contagion as follows:
"Early in July an old soldier entered
the home for a short lest and soon aft- ,
erward appeared at the dispensary, !
where he informed the physician in
charge that he was but recently from
Santiago via transport to a northern
port; thence he started for Manila via
San Francisco; beat his way to the
home on a freight train and entered
with his baggage. He complained of
dumb chills and fever and was pre
scribed for. He mingled freely with \
the inmates of the home and a short j
time ago disappeared. This is regarded j
as a possible use of the outbreak. i
"Preceding Friday, the 21st, there J
had been re. thing of a suspicious na
ture. The weekly death rate was as
f usual. But on the "Ist and 22d there
became ill during the night, with sharp
chills and high fever, some eight or ten ;
inmates, men of usually good health. I
These seizures, although noted as pecu- j
liar, had occasioned no alarm until i
about Thursday, the- 27th, when a death ;
occurred^ the man becoming distinctly]
yellow some time prior to his death. On
or about the same day another died
without having attracted particular at- â 
tention. He Iso turned quite yellow.
Autopsies were held on both by the
resident patologist. It was then, or a';
little later, that the telegram was sent
asking for ar . expert. I found there
were 3600 inmates and that 35 had been
taken ill since the 21st." Dr. Wadin
says he withheld a positive diagnosis j

Special Dispatch to The Call.
CLEVELAND, July Whether the :
boycott on the Big Consolidated Street j
Railway Company is having any effect or
not, the company shows no indication of
a desire to change its policy with respect
to the strike. A special meeting of the |
directors of the company was held this
afternoon. At Its conclusion President
Henry A. Everett was asked if the strike
had been discussed.
"Incidentally it was." was his reply.
"Is it the Intention of the company to
move for a settlement of the strike?" was
asked. â¢ â¢'.
"No." said President Everett. "We are
going ahead as .we have been going. We
are hiring new men all the time, and we
now have nearly all we want. Some of
the new men arc being discharged when
found to be not the mem we want and
others put in their places. We are satis
fled with the situation, and 1 might say
that the board of directors % has been a
unit from the beginning, and lt is unani
mous in favor of the policy being pur
sued." â .'â¢-
On the other hand, the leaders of the
strike profess to be very much encouraged
by the working of the boycott, -and they
say they must surely win, because, they
declare, the public is with them. The
president of the union has been busy all
day listening to the complaints -of mer
chants and others who claim they have
been boycotted without warrant, and
when he discovers that the person who
"complains is . Innocent of the charge of
having ridden on a Big Consolidated car
he gives notice to union men accordingly.
Secretary Bishop of the State Board of
Arbitration arrived in the city to-day to
look over the situation. He said he was
willing to undertake an investigation of
the strike if it was desired. He had a
conference with President Everett, but
refused to say what transpired. It Is In
timated that he may visit the strike lead
ers in the morning.

. perialism in the United
* . . States. _ '..
LONDON, Aug. I.â A dispatch to the
Morning Post from Rome mentions a re
port current there to the effect that the
larger South American republics are sus
picious of the United States' absorption
of Cuba, Porto Rico and the Philippines
and the growth of imperialism in Amer
ica and have exchanged views on the sub

against the United States.
The prime, movers in negotiations are
Brazil, Argentina, Uruguay and Para
guay.
It is said that preliminaries for an alli
ance have all been concluded between
General Julio Roca, President of the Ar
gentine Republic, and Dr. Campos Salles,
President Of Brazil. After the formation
of the league, Bolivia, Peru, Colombia,
Ecuador and Venezuela will join it.

HAMPTON, July 31.- This usually
tranquil town has been in a state of
feverish excitement to-day since the
discovery of yellow fever at the Sol
diers' Home. ires of people took ad
vantage of the first opportunity to
leave the place. Last -.light a speciai

guests of the hotels.
Business in the town of Phoebus, ad- ;

Winchester rifles, vent to the home to

ulations. Chesapeake and Ohio trains
have discontinued their service to the j
city.
NEWPORT NEWS, July 31.â After a j
day of intense excitement over the yel- i
low fever outbreak at the Soldiers' i
Home, Newport. News rests to-night
with a feeling of comparative security.
It is felt now that no loophole has been
left by which the scourge may find its
way to this city.
Newport News has quarantined
against Hampton and Elizabeth City
and county and Warrick County. Thus
this city, which is nearly ten miles
from the infected point, has the ad
vantage of practically a double quaran
tine on land. A tugboat patrols the
river to prevent the evasion of quar
antine restrictions by means of boats.
The danger line ashore is guarded by
an adequate force of special policemen.
The street railway company was pro
hibited this morning from running cars
between Hampton and this city.
Hundreds of people left the. city to
day on account of the scare.
â NORFOLK.. July Fear -of the

miles of water between Norfolk and the
Soldiers' Home, and the Norfolk River
front is constantly guarded by police
men, while a guard from the navy yard
is stationed at the naval hospital to
prevent the landing of small boats con
taining refugees from the peninsula.
The Old Point hotels are practically de.
serted.
ISLAND GRAIN
FIELDS ABLAZE
Losses by San Joaquin
Farmers.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
STOCKTON. July 31.â Grain fires swept ;
through the harvest-laden fields of the
lowlands this afternoon. Roberts Island
farmers were the losers. Three thousand
acres were threatened and for an hour
anxious watchers observed the efforts be
ing made to save the vast fortunes at
stake. . There was no close telephone com
munication and numbers went to the
Courthouse dome to watch the scene
through glasses.
A dense volume of smoke passed over
the. southern portion of the City shortly
after -2 o'clock, and it was at once sur
mised that the daily conflagration had
started on the islands. The lire burned
for two hours, and during all that time
the farm laborers, reinforced by thresh
ing outfits, worked like men whose lives
were at stake. They plowed around and
beat the flames, and at 4 o'clock were
rewarded by getting the fire under con
trol.
Two hundred acres were burned over
and many thousands threatened. Woods
Bros, are the heaviest losers, over half
of the burned area belonging to them.
; The fire started- from a corner of the
Blossom ranch, three miles from the old
Garwood ferry.
There was also a fire on the island near
Fishermans Camp yesterday, by which
Lane & Thompson are reported to have
lost about 200 acres.
Peat fires are now known to have been
the, cause of all the recent destruction
of grain ;on the islands. The under
ground coal beds are becoming a really
serious consideration, after several years
of slumbering, following the overflowing
lof the lands. Farmers with fine crops
! do not know at what minute a blaze is
going to break out, and some measures
must be taken to quench the peat fire
beds* The farmers are going to flood their
lands where possible, while others will
'â  ditch around the subterranean fires.

LOS ANGELES, " July 31. â Teodore

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY. AUGUST 1, 1399.
STATE PENAL CODE
A BOOK OF ERRORS
Report of the Revision
Commission.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, July 31.â The commis
j sion for. the revision and reform of the
I law, consisting of A. Caminetti and R. N.
i Bulla, to-day submitted to Secretary of
] State Curry volume 3 of the report of the ;
. commission, the volume being the revised
i Penal Code of California. Says the com
' mission's report in part:
I A glance at the law under which the com
mission has acted will soon determine that
â¢ the main reason for its enactment was founded
on the long-felt want of legislators and State,
' count, and other officers generally, as well as
. the judiciary and members of the bar, to ob- ;
tain, not. so much new legislation, as a revis- |
ion and codification of existing laws.
j This want was no doubt based on the indis
! criminate passage of statutes ' rather than ,
amendments to codes on the .subjects contained ,
in the latter since their adoption.
This practice had grown to such an extent
that fully one-third of the political code was
/obsolete; the civil code touching on many sub- :
jects lacked many of the laws affecting them, j
especially a? to corporations; the penal code j
was minus a considerable portion of the penal
enactments in force, and the civil procedure,
though less affected, was also ln need of re
vision.
The only changes adopted were such as were
required in changing a statute standing alone
Into a section, article or chapter, forming a
component part of a codified system on the
subject to which it related, and such as might
be necessary to remove portions thereof which
had been repealed, or were declared unconsti
tutional by the courts. Conflicting provisions
were harmonized, and- existing laws containing
uncertain and Incongruous provisions, which
had been tne subject of consideration by the
Supreme Court, were corrected in accordance ,
with the opinion of that tribunal.
Investigations have been carried on by mem
bers of the commission and material gathered
which would have permitted us to complete
the entire work of the first division by the
first Of October, 1899.
. The civil code was transmitted to the last
Legislature and the Judiciary Committee of the
Assembly, acting upon the recommendation of
a sub-committee appointed to examine said re
port, ordered the first volume introduced as a
bill by the Judiciary Committee.
We have since reviewed the political code,
1 adding thereto the legislation properly forming
i part thereof, and adopted at the last legisla
i tive session, and filed the same June 30 of the
present year. ."*''.
On this day we have filed a supplemental re
port consisting of the revised civil code, con 7
raining the amendments thereto of said session.
The' original of this report was filed November
80, 1898 â  -.-**-"
The commission, in carrying on the work of
revision above mimed, have examined tie exist
ing laws in and out of the codes. This Involved
thousands Of code sections and statutes, ami
the determination of many questions arising
from such investigation.

SAMPSON FILES SUIT
FOR HIS PRIZE MONEY
Libels the Maria Teresa and Other
Spanish Vessels Taken by
His Fleet.
WASHINGTON. July 31.-Rear Admiral
William T. Sampson has filed suit in the
Supreme Court of the District libeling the
Spanish vessel Maria Teresa and miscella
neous-stores and supplies captured upon
her and other Spanish vessels. In his bill
he says that he brings the suit in his
own behalf and also in behalf of all the
officers and enlisted -men of the United
States navy who served with the United
States naval force and took part in the
naval engagement of Santiago de Cuba
on July 3. 1 SOS . and in captures made sub
sequent thereto. . __ -.
Admiral Sampson says that the fleet of
the United States was acting under his
immediate command as commander-in
chief; that Commodore Winfield S. Schley
was the commanding officer- of a division
of the fleet under his orders and Captain
French E. Chad wick was fleet captain, or
chief of staff. He recites the capture of
the Spanish vessels, and says that the
captured property has been or will be sur
veyed, appraised and Inventoried, adding:
"This will show that the ' Teresa, was
worth $700,000 .over and above the cost of
recovery." â _ ..
He asks a rule upon the Secretary of the
Navy to show why the value of the cap
tured property shown, or which may be
shown by the survey, appraisement or in
ventory, should not be deposited with the
Treasurer or Assistant Treasurer of the
United' States, subject to the order of the
court in v this case. .

Men Who Have Lost Their Lives in
the Gold Fields.
VICTORIA, B. C, July 31.â Dawson
advices note the arrival there on July
9 of a party that started over the Ed
monton Trail fourteen months ago. The
party consisted, of Captain George
Dunn, Edward Miller, Alfred B. Clegg,
William Brownlow, James Wallwork,
Charles Fowler, Hugh Stevens and
Henry Jackson. They endured terrible
hardships. It is the first party that has
made the trip from the starting point
on the Mackenzie River in the same
boat.
The Yukon Council has decided to
construct a series of public highways.
Work is to be commenced without de
lay. Already $10,000 has been appro
priated for the work, and as much
more has been pledged by mine owners.
Substantial wagon roads will be con
structed to all the principal creeks.

THROWN FROM A CARRIAGE.
Mother and Child Narrowly . Escape
Death at Santa Cruz.
SANTA CRUZ, July Mrs. George
Tait met a serious accident yesterday
afternoon, and her 2-year-old child had a
narrow escape from being killed. Mrs
Tait was on her way to the depot, riding
in a carriage. The horse got frightened at
an electric car. Mrs. Tait jumped from
the carriage and fell, between the wheels
which ran over her. breaking an ankle.'
The baby was thrown under the horse,
i and William Hanley grabbed it just in
! time to save its life.

Turned On the Gas.
MANY CENSURE,
OTHERS DEFEND
Character of McNaugh-
ton Dissected.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, July 31.â The investigation !
into the charges of immorality, incom
petency and lack of character made
against Professor James McNaughton,
the new principal of the State Normal
School, by a committee of the Alumni
Association commenced before the board
of trustees this morning. The full board
was present Trustees H. C. Brown
(chairman). G. W. Sweigert, R. T. Pierce,
F. J. Short and F. W. Leavitt. and State
Superintendent of Instruction Kirk. Gov
ernor Gage, who is an ex-offlcio member
of the board and is said to have been re
sponsible for McNaughton being made
principal, did not appear.
Attorney J. R. Patton represented Mc-
Naughton. Eli Wright and J. G. Jury,
members . of the Investigating committee
and attorneys, represented the Alumni
Association.
As soon as Chairman Brown announced
all in readiness Eli Wright presented a
supplementary report to the report filed
at the last meeting of the board. It was
a voluminous document, consisting of let
ters received in relation to McNaughton,
and occupied over two hours in reading.
There were letters from business men,
teachers and pupils of Council Bluffs,
Iowa; Mayville, N. D., and Temple, Ariz.
A great many of them related to Mc-
Naughton's record at Council Bluffs and
referred to the charges of immorality and
lack of character made against Mc-
Naughton while he was Superintendent of
Schools there. '_ '"â *;â 'â *Â«
A letter from S. B. Wadsworth, an attor
ney at Council Bluffs, said he firmly be
lieved the charges made against Mc-
Naughton to be true. McNaughton was
incompetent. He loaned money on chattel
securities, and notorious characters called
at the school buildings and McNaughton
took them into the basement and trans
acted business. By loaning money to dis
reputable women he enabled them to con
tinue their immoral practices. There were
other letters of this kind.
The correspondence from Tempe, where
McNaughton last taught, related princi
pally to his incompetency as teacher and
disciplinarian. He was accused of favor
itism, and the charge was made that he
had accepted $20 from a pupil in consid
eration for allowing him to graduate. A
few letters spoke of him as a scholar and
a gentleman. .: _Â,,Â»- i-
Attorney Patton followed in McNaugh
ton's defense. He read a typewritten
statement consisting of 200 pages and
consumed nearly three hours It reflect
ed on the investigating committee of the
alumni to the effect that it was unauthor
ized and did not â¢ represent the Alumni
Associatiqn. Letters were read from
ministers, educators and prominent citi
zens giving McNaughton a high char
acter arid denouncing the attack on him
as malicious. The action of persons in
Council Bluffs in sending out what were
termed anonymous charges was de
nounced as worse than highway robbery,
as it was an attempt under cover to rob
Professor McNaughton of his good repu
tation. _ â â¢.-"â¢â  '-:.'." _ . _ 'â 
The animus of the charge against Mc-
Naughton was declared to be the fact
that Superintendent McNaughton waa
Instrumental in the dismissal of a drunk
en and incompetent teacher named Arthur
Stevens. It was claimed the charges were
due to malice and revenge and had no
foundation in fact, and Professor Mc-
Naughton. had been given no opportunity
to deny them. Ministers of all the
churches of Council Bluffs had testified
to McNaughton's good character.
Resolutions from the trustees of the
Mayville (N. D.) Normal were read, de
claring that after an investigation they
were satisfied the charges made at
Council Bluffs were not true.
Attorney Jury argued for thirty
minutes. He urged the necessity of the
dismissal of Professor McNaughton. A
man of his character would drag down
the school, even if his innocence were
proved. â¢-.-''â .'.â¢'
The investigation will be resumed in the
morning, when Attorney Patton will
close f-Â«r the defense. The case will then
be submitted to the board, which will
probably take it under advisement.
The normal trustees met this evening
and reinstated Miss Cornelia Walker on
the petition of a large number of citizens.
At the annual election she was dropped.
Her position will be assigned by the presi
dent. The resignation of Miss Louise '
Maitland as teacher of drawing was ac- I
cepted. _______________ â

DU PATY DE CLAM
REGAINS LIBERTY
Sruguiere to Counter
sign His Acquittal.
â â¦ â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PARIS, July 31.â !.â - Soir announces
that Colonel Dv Paty de Clam was
liberated at 3 o'clock this afternoon.
He immediately drove to his home and
retired to bed, alleging excessive fa
tigue. He declined to see anybody.
General Bruguiere, Military Governor
of Paris, has the power to override the
decision that there is no case against
Colonel Dv Paty de Clam, but it is un
derstood that he will countersign the
acquittal.
Nothing Is yet known of the actual
grounds of the acquittal, but it is re
ported that Dv Paty de Clam was ex
onerated because it was proved that he
acted under direct orders from Gen
erals Gonz and De Boisdeffre, against
whom severe disciplinary measures are
imminent. If this is confirmed it will
prove that the efforts of tie- general
staff to make General Dv Paty de Clam
a scapegoat have failed.
General Roget and Major Culgnet,
who were tie chief witnesses against
Dreyfus in the proceedings in the Court
of Cassation, each maintains that he
has absolute proof of Dv Paty de
Clam's forgeries and intrigues. If
their statements, which it was believed
at the time were made to shield the
general staff, are discredited, nothing
will remain against Dreyfus.
RENNES, July 31.â Already the
crowds that gather daily about the
home of Mme. Dreyfus and the prison
! whore her husband is confined, as she
passes from one to the other, have be
come so augmented as to demand a
material increase in the force policing
the route. Gendarmes therefore have
been placed at close Intervals along the
entire distance from the Rue de Cha
tillon. where Mme. Dreyfus resides, to
the prison, In the Rue dv Hamel. Strict
orders have been issued to prevent per
sons passing from the Rue dv Hamel to
the prison precincts. This has resulted
in a number of conflicts with the
gendarmes, none of which, however,
has been serious.

REVOLT ABOUT TO
BE PROCLAIMED
Crisis Reached in San
Domingo.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CAPE HAYTIEN. Hayti, July 31.â From
a dispatch just received from a reliable
source it is learned that a revolution will
be proclaimed to-morrow or the day after
to-morrow in the republic of San Domingo
in favor of Don Juan Isidor Jiminez.
According to the dispatch the entire
western portion of the republic has de
clared in favor of Jiminez, and he is the
only candidate for the Presidency
throughout the rest of the country.
FORT DE FRANCE, Island of Martin
ique, July 31.â News is arriving slowly,
owing to the occurrence in San Domingo
on Friday last of a violent hurricane
which caused immense damage. Three
large schooners which were In the road
stead of San Domingo were wrecked, and
only one man of the crews of the vessels
was saved.
After striking San Domingo the hur
ricane moved to the northeast, devastat
ing the country between San Domingo
and Coin, forty-four miles from the capi
tal. The extent of the damage is not
known.
The latest advices from San Domingo
report that General Wenceslayo Figuero,
the Vice President, urged by a number
of his friends, has accepted the Presi
dency, promising to retain the present
conservative Ministry in office, Pending
the meeting of Congress, General Figuero
will assume full responsibility and power.
Only In the environs of Moca is the
agitation caused by the assassins and
their friends apparent, and it is said that
the partisans of the Government are in
creasing in numbers.
The Governor of Moca has taken meas
ures for the preservation of order and
great activity Is being displayed by the
troops of the garrison. Active prepara
tions arc being made with a view to sur
rounding the enemy and to repulse an at
tack which it is thought they may make
at any moment. '"V
At San Lorenzo de Guayubin the Gover
nor has appealed to all the people of the
city -.o pledge themselves to co-operate
iii the maintenance of order. Commerce
is paralyzed throughout the country.
LONDON, August I.â The Dally Chron
icle's Rome correspondent says: The
newspapers announce that in view of pos
sible (-implications in Central America the
Italian squadron has started from Buenos
Ayres. There has been an interchange of
views between the Cabinets of the great
powers with reference to the probability
of a civil war in San Domingo and
eventual intervention by the United
States. __________Â«__Â».

Big Catches of Salmon.
ASTORIA, July 31.â The run of salmon
has Increased and fishermen are aver-'
aging 800 pounds to the boat. They are

big wages. It is thought the run will con

ends. If so the pack will probably be not
more than 50,000 cases short.

MYSTERIOUS NO
LONGER IS THE
HERRIN GUEST
Reason of Steele's
Visit Here.
At last the presence of the mysterious
Steele at the Palace Hotel is explained.
Mr. Steele, who Is a member of Seward,
Guthrie & Steele, one of the leading law
firms of New York, arrived here a few
days ago under circumstances which
caused not a little comment. He was
brought here by Attorney Herrin of the
Southern Pacific, who journeyed to. Den- I
ver in a private car for the purpose of j
meeting and escorting his distinguished j
guest. Upon the arrival in this city Mr. ,
Steele was assigned to rooms in tin- Pal
ace Hotel without having to go through
the formality of putting his name on the
hotel books. He brought a stenographer
with him, who was kept busy until all
hours of the night. He denied himself to
reporters. Mr. Herrin professed to be en
tirely ignorant of his identity, and alto
gether the whole affair savored of in
trigue and romance and gave its chief
actor a character that would have stood
for his being anything from a foreign po
tentate to a domestic train robber.
The fact of the matter . is that Mr.
Steele's' presence here was for the purpose
of consulting with Herrin and arranging
the details of the big deal which is fully
explained in the following dispatch from
Salt Lake:
SALT LAKE. Utah, July 31.â Cen
tral Pacific Railway Company was incor
porated here to-day for the purpose of ac
quiring all the railroad land, land grants
and franchises of the Central Pacific Rail
road. The company is incorporated in
pursuance of the plan of reorganization
of the Central Pacific, and is in the Inter
est of the Southern Pacific
Thomas Mar. of Utah is named as
president of the new corporation, whose
capital stock is placed at $87,000,000.
REV. J. C. LYNN OF
SAN JOSE RESIGNS
The Weil-Known Divine Leaves the
United Presbyterian Church for
a Larger Field.
At a called meeting of the San Fran
cisco Presbytery at San Jose yesterday
the Rev. John C. Lynn, for the past three
years pastor of the United Presbyterian
Church of San Jose, tendered his resig
nation.
This course was made necessary by rea
son of the action of the committee on
i Home Missions, which met at Philadel
phia. Pa., in May last. For the past
twenty-four years the Home Mission
Board has supported ln part the San Jose
Church, but this year it failed to grant it
: any aid. thus leaving the pastor without
j a living salary.
The Rev. Mr. Lynn has rendered faith
-1 ful service and his work has been cmi
i nently successful. The action of the board
is not for the purpose of dismissing Dr.
Lynn, but to stimulate the church to self
\ support. Dr. Lynn will either go East,
'. where several calls await him, or take up
â  the work at Agenda, a new and flourish
j ing mission near Salinas.

WASHINGTON AWAITS
THE SAMOAN TREATY
Meanwhile Every One, Including Officials, Is Marveling
Over the Call's Great Scoop.
NEW YORK, July 31.â The Call's great scoop on the Samoan treaty
was all the talk of the town on Park Row last night, or rather
early this morning. The correspondents, who sit up for early
morning papers, discussed this scoop and speculated on the way
The Call managed to secure the big piece of exclusive news. All of the
morning papers reprinted the treaty and the report Of the commission,
and all gave credit to The. Call except the Journal. This paper put a big
head over the news, and printed it in full under San Francisco date, and
introduced the dispatch by saying "The Samoan treaty was received
here to-day," but . never a word about The Call.
In the Herald office, the big scoop was generally discussed and the
enterprise of The Call was highly commended.
The Call's correspondent at Washington sends the following:
"The newspapers, have scored a scoop on the department," said Sec
retary Hay to-day, when asked for an expression of opinion on the de
tailed report of the Samoan commission published in The Call Sunday
and in other papers this morning. The Secretary playfully indulged in
newspaper slang and at the same time-assured me that he was officially
ignorant of the findings of the commission. He did not attempt to dis
credit the report as published in the morning papers, but he gracefully
declined to discuss the subject or make any comment upon the report of
the commission until an official copy of that document is before him.
The Secretary explained that the administration has the greatest confi
dence in the wisdom, ability and discretion of the members of the com
mission and their recommendations or suggestions will now naturally be
received with full force. He added that the official report arrived in
San Francisco by the same steamer' which brought the published report
and suggested that it may be on its way to Washington by mail.
In diplomatic circles there was but little disposition to comment
upon the report, because few of the responsible legation men are in
Washington at this time. There are some differences of opinion as to the
wisdom of forming a council composed of delegates from each power
with an administrator from a neutral nation.
It is believed by many persons that the problem will not be finally
solved- until either the United States. Great Britain or Germany takes
absolute possession of Samoa. It is contended that friction will continue
so long as the existing three-cornered responsibility prevails.
DEATH OF A ONCE
FAMOUS BEAUTY
Close of the Career of Mrs. Kate
Chase Sprague, Who Was Well
K.oÂ° wn in Washiogton.
WASHINGTON, July 31.â Mrs.
Kate Chase Sprague, wife of
former Governor Sprague of
Rhode Island and the daugh
ter of the late Salmon P.
! Chase, Governor of Ohio, United States
i Senator and Chief Justice of the Su-
p reme Court, died at her homestead.
Edgewood, In Washington suburbs,
early this morning. She was 59 years
I old. For three months she had been ill,
l but had consented to medical treatment
i only ten days ago. She grew steadily-
I worse and the end came a few minutes
after 3 o'clock this morning. At the
bedside were her three daughters. Miss
I Kittie Sprague, who lived with her
: mother; Miss Portia Sprague of Nar
> ragansett Pier, and Mrs. Donaldson of
Brooklyn, N. Y.
Not many American women have had
a more varied career than did the beau
i tiful and brilliant daughter of Salmon - .
1 Chase once the mistress of millions, then
dependent on the generosity of the friends
of her father and her own for mainten
ance and to save the roof under which he
died to shelter herself and children. The
daughter of a man whose name was a
synonym for all that men esteem as noble
and virtuous, and on which she herself in
yeara of prominence In the highest social
'life Of the capital and the country but
shed additional luster, and the wife of a
man almost as prominent and as much

Sheridan. Senator Sherman and others
also went to talk of old times and dis- |

a year on her by her father's and her own
friends put an end to the financial ruin
that had long threatened her; and with

able traits, to restore the fortunes of her |

well. Yet the spectacle of the beautiful

HIS WIFE LEFT HIM
AND HE TOOK HIS LIFE
Thomas Lapsley, an upholsterer, re
siding at 790 Harrison street, near Fourth,
committed suicide early yesterday morn
ing at the house of his friend, William
Archer. 417%. Fifth street. I.apsley quar
reled with his wife and separated from
her some time ago. Ever since the sep
aration he had been despondent, and on
Sunday he told Miss Kennedy, his land
lady's daughter, that he intended to kill
himself. After he left the house he be.
came intoxicatedâ something quite un
usual for himâ went to the residence
of his friend. Mr. Archer, on Sunday
night. Archer put him to bed and left
him to sleep off his fit.
At 7 o'clock yesterday morning when
Mr. Archer went to the room to wake
I.apsley he found him dead. The body
was yet warm, but there was nothing iii
the room to indicate the manner in
which Lapsley had committed suicide. A
small bottle of a bromo preparation for
the cure of headache was found in a
pocket of the dead man, but the vial had
not been opened.
On the front and back of an envelope
Lapsley bad scrawled in leadDencll some
almost unintelligible line... All that could
be deciphered was the following:
To Those Whom It May Concernâ I.
Thomas Lapsley, hereby take the life that 1
earned through my most forgiven acts In this
world. What a miserable world It Is! Every
body is for himself, but I am. for one, for
everybody. No more. â¢ â¢ â¢ My friends

Dewey Says the Victory at Manila
Was Largely Due to Ent
wistle's Excellent Man
agement.
Â» â 
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. WELLING
TON HOTEL. WASHINGTON, July 31.
From commander to rear-admiral in less
than six months is the remarkably quick
jump made by James Entwlstle, chief en
gineer cf Admiral Dewey's fleet, as a re
j sult of the action just taken by the Navy
| Department in ordering his retirement,
; with the rank and three-quarters sea pay
|of his next highest grade. By the oper
| ation of the navy personnel law Rear Ad
i miral Entwlstle was transferred to the
! line of the navy with the rank of cap
! tain. By a provision of the same law of
j ficers who served in the war of the re
bellion are entitled to retire with the rank
lof the next highest grade. The grade of
I commodore having been abolished by the
â¢same law, rear admiral is the next highest
I grade to that which Entwlstle obtained
;by his transfer to the line. Hence his pro
i motion to the rank of rear admiral.
Entwlstle was in the navy during the
Civil War and has served Uncle Sam on
the seas ever since. When the war was
declared with Spain he had risen to be
fleet engineer with Dewey's squadron. He
was on the Olympia at the admiral's side
, during the battle, and his promptitude
: was such that he was complimented after
-1 ward by Dewey. The great commander
I declared, too. that the faultless condition
of the machinery throughout the fleet
and the excellent management of Entwls
tle were largely responsible for the wip
! ing out of the Spaniards.
Admiral Dewey furthermore referred In
i a very complimentary manner to Entwls
â¢! tie in a speech which he made in Aus
: tria. He must have spoken at headquar
ters as well, for it was not long after
the great battle that a commission as
captain arrived for Entwlstle.
Captain Entwistle was near the ago
limit and was sent to his home in Pater
son N. J., with the expectation of being
retired He is spending the summer at
. Lake George and received no information.
; of the action in his case until a few days
! asro Then he had a letter from Acting
Secretary Allen of the Navy Department
informing him of his retirement under the
: provisions of the personnel bill. The let
ter also informed Captain Entwistle that
he would hereafter have the rank and.
three-quarters of the sea pay of a rear
3 Rear Admiral Untwistle will finish the
' summer at the lake and thinks^ he may
- afterward be assigned to shore duty. Ha
has just received a fine medal of the sort
ordered by Congress for all the heroes oC
Manila.
Silver Paramount, Says Teller.
SEATTLE. July 31.â United States Sen
ator Teller of Colorado arrived here to
night from Vancouver. B. C. Discussing
national politics. Senator Teller said Mc-
Kinlev and Bryan would be the opposing
candidates in 1900 and the money question
would be the paramount issue. As to the
Philippine question, he thought that the
United States should maintain control
over the Islands.

Suicide in a Cemetery.
SEATTLE. July 31.â body of Jesse
K. Cowles of St. Paul, Minn., was found
in the G. A. R. cemetery this morning.
He had committed suicide with laudanum.
Cowles was about 30 years old and came
to this city about two weeks ago. His
failure to obtain employment Is assigned
as the cause of his suicide.

THE ABBOTT FIRST
IN STRAIGHT HEATS
Easily Wins the 2:08 Trot on the
Opening Day of the Columbus
Meeting.
COLUMBUS. Ohio. July 31.â The Grand
Circuit meeting opened to-day under the
Biost favorable conditions. The weather
was clear and the track fast, though it
will improve considerably with continued
fair weather. There were three races on
the card and, with the exception of the
2:23 pace, they were almost featureless.
The big race of the day was the 2:08
trot, with four startersâ The Abbott,
Eagle Flanagan, Kentucky Union and
Mattle Patterson. The Abbott was almost
a prohibitive favorite, and the bay gelding
did not disappoint his backers, winning in
straight heats. Eagle Flanagan was ex
pected to give The Abbott a brush, but
the favorite won with ease, being crowded
only in the second and third heats. The
Abbott clipped a quarter of a second off
his record in the second heat, when he
went the mile in 2:07%.
In the 2:14 pace Hob Fitzsimmons won in
straight heats, taking the first In the fast
time of 2:07%. There were nine starters In
this race, but Bob Fitzsimmons was al
most a. prohibitive favorite.
The 2:23 pace furnished the only excite
ment of the day. There were seven start
ers. Although Shade On was picked by
many to win. Wandering Jew sold favor
ite in the pools and his backers did not
flinch when Shade On landed the first
heat. Only four horses remained in the
race after the first heat- Wandering Jew,
Shade On, Beauty Spot and Mount Clem
ens Boy. The Jew took the second and
third heats in apparently easy style and
looked a sure winner, but in the fourth
he broke several times and showed evi
dence of weakening, dropping Into fourth
â¢place, while- Shade On took the heat.
When the horses scored for the fifth heat
it was evident that the Jew was out of it.
He nearly fell just after he passed the
wire and trailed far behind the bunch,
being shut out of second money by the
distance flag. There was a sharp brush
between Shade On and Mount Clemens
Boy at the finish, but the former won by
a length under the whip. Results:

third. Kentucky Union also started.

111.

and Constance, respectively Canadian de

JONES DEFEATS HARPER.
Recovers the Tennis Championship
of Monterey County.
DEL. MONTE, July 31.â James Harper,
holder of the tennis singles championship
of Monterey County for 1898, and A. W.
Jones Jr., winner of the '99 county tour
nament,, played a match for the county
championship of the year 1899 on the main
court at this place this afternoon. The
play was fast throughout, each man doing
splendid work. Harper won the first set
in good style, the score being 6âl, but
Jones took the next three sets by scores
of 7â5, 6 â &â4. Leon Greenebaum of San
Francisco umpired the match, John Wil
ley of Pacific Grove acting as scorer.
Jones held the county championship for
three years, losing to Harper in 1597, since
which the latter has successfully defend
ed the title until to-day.
Matthews Defeats Connolly.
NEW YORK, July 31.â Mattie Mat
thews of New York defeated Eddie Con
nolly of St. Johns in a twenty-flve-round
bout before the Coney Island Sporting
Club to-night. They met at 138 pounds,
and at the end of the mill the referee,
George Edwards, decided that Matthews
had won. This ruling was not well re
ceived by many of the spectators, who
hissed and yelled at the referee, but Mat
thews had clearly the better of it. hav
ing administered twice as much punish
ment as he received. Jerry Marshall of
Australia defeated Bob Allen of Boston
In a twenty-round bout.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 1. 1899.
BICYCLE RIDERS
PAY LIGHT FINES
Round- Up of Wheelmen
in San Mateo.
â Â»
ENFORCING AN ORDINANCE
â  â¦ â 
USE OF BELLS AND LAMPS i
INSISTED UPON.
4)
Redwood City Residents Spend a
Day Watching the Bringing
In of the Luckless
Pedalers.
â â â¦
Special Dispatch to The Call.
REDWOOD CITY, July Bicycle ;
riders from neighboring counties would do
well to obtain a copy of the ordinance
passed some time ago by the Board ot i
Supervisors of San Mateo County and j
carefully study Its provisions. The or- I
dinance makes it a misdemeanor to ride
at any time without a bell, and at night,
from sunset to sunrise of the following
morning, without a lamp. It is an offense
to ride with the feet off the pedals, gen
erally known as "coasting," or to drag
behind the wheel any bush, stick or other
article.
The ordinance took effect .some time
ago, but the officers were lenient with
riders, refraining from making arrests to I
give wheelmen an opportunity to become
acquainted with Its provisions. Yester- \
day, however, they commenced a crusade I
against bicyclists violating the ordinance,
and as a resull a large number of arrests I
were made. Constable Chatham and his
deputies were busy most of the day run
ning wheelmen Into the Justice's Court.
As a result the treasury of the county la ;
richer and the bicyclists poorer.
As a rule the wheelmen took their ar- :
rest in good part, pleaded guilty, paid
their tines and went on their way. Others :
swore vengeance upon the constable and
court for transacting business on a day j
of rest, but finally followed the example
of the more philosophical and paid their;
fines. One pleaded "Not guilty," demand
ed a trial and put up a $". cash bail. It ls .
understood this one took the boat for Ore- j
gon this morning, forfeiting his bail.
The fines were light and considerable ;
amusement was supplied the townspeople,
who had nothing In particular to do but i
watch the fun. From now on a firm com- !
pliance with the ordinance will be de- ,
manded, and violators cannot expect le- j
niency from the court if convicted.

THE OIL INDUSTRY
RECEIVES A BOOM
AN EXCHANGE IS TO BE ESTAB-
LISHED IN THIS CITY.
A Meeting of Bepresentative Men in
the Palace Hotel Results in the
Starting of a Big
Concern.
A meeting of well-known gentlemen was
held last evening in apartments in the
Palace Hotel for the purpose of forming
an organization that has for its object the
establishment of a permanent oil ex
change In this city.
San Francisco, being the natural mar
ket for the oil production of this State,
it was decided some time ago to place an
exchange founded on the same lines as
those now in operation in Pittsburg, New
York and London.
The exchange will, through a system of
certificates very much the same as those
used in other exchanges, handle the entire
oil business of the State, thus greatly fa
cilitating the growth of the Industry and
affording great assistance to both the
buyer and the seller. All data and infor
mation pertaining to oil will be collected
and filed away for the reference and in
formation of those interested. As the.
business increases stocks will be listed
and a call board established which will
provide for a president, vice president,
treasurer, secretary and chairman and
the oil business of this State will assume
the same Importance as the wheat pit or
the mining exchange.
Tho meeting last night was only for the
purpose of getting those interested in the
enterprise together and effecting a tem
porary organization which, it Is expect
ed, will be merged Into a permanent one
somewhere about the Ist of next Septem
ber. To conduct the business of the tem
porary association the following officers
were elected: Wendell Easton, president;
Henry Crocker, treasurer; Emil E. Kahn,
secretary. ">-:.'
The association, which is an entirely
voluntary one and unincorporated, is lim
ited to fifty members, of whom the follow
ing were present to affix their names to
the by-laws: Wendell Easton, Henry J.
Crocker, Emll E. Kahn, August Water
man, Werner Stauf, R. H. Herron. E.
Gauthier. George F. Beveridge, W. H. H.
Hart, Max Wolf, P. L. Wooster, J. Kahn,
G. Â£>â¢ Easton, Leon Phlpps, Jesse W.
Lllienthal, H. C. Bunker, Hermann Na
than of Fresno, J. J. Mack of Bakersfield.
Fred W. McNear, C. B. Stone. A. B.
Smith of Fresno, George D. Moore.
DOXEY IS ON
TOE VERGE OF
BANKRUPTCY
May Be Declared an
Insolvent To-Day.
â¦ â
VICTIM OF KEEN COMPETITION
â â¢ â
HE IS UNABLE TO MEET LIABILI
TIES NOW DUE.
1,
For the Past Week the Bookseller Has
Been Trying to Realize Money
by Selling His Books at
a Low Figure.
â¢*: â
William Doxey, the well-known book
seller and publisher, Is again in a state
of financial embarrassment, and in all
probability he will to-day petition to be
declared insolvent.
For the past week Doxey has been in
dustriously getting rid of his magnificent
stock of literary works at low figures,
and there are now many empty book
shelves in his spacious store under the
Palace. 7,. 7 7
Doxey's financial troubles were first
aired last January, and at that time his
creditors held a meeting and generously
decided to allow him to continue business,
it being agreed that he should meet some
of his liabilities at that time and others
to-day. This settlement for liabilities
amounting to $48,300 was made on a basis
of 30 cents on the dollar, 15 per cent of
the total amount being due to-day and the
balance six months hence.
Mr, Doxey states that keen competition
is mainly responsible for his financial
ruin. "Selling books at a 20 per cent dis
count will ruin anybody," said Mr. Doxey
to a CaU reporter last night. "Ever since
I first was forced to have an understand
ing with my creditors business has been
unusually bad. I have struggled hard to
keep my It ad above water, and I found
it a rather hard task. The sale which
has been in progress here for the last
week has brought in little money. I
thought that I might be able to realize
quite a sum, but I had to let the books go
at a sacrifice. The money market is dull
and the people haven't got any money.
"I will decide to-morrow what my fu
ture course will be. Whether or not I will
to-morrow petition to be declared insol
vent I am not prepared to say to-night.
I will have reached a conclusion by morn
ing."
Deep concern is felt for Mr. Doxey un
fortunate predicament. He has been one
of the landmarks of the local business
world and has hosts of sympathizers In
commercial circles.
MONEY FOR TEACHERS.
Funds Will Be Divided Between Them
and the Merchants.
As a result of the decision rendered by '
Judge Hebbard last Friday that the Mis
sion High School fund was not a separ- '
ate special fund, and could be used for i
general school purposes, teachers and
merchants who had claims under the old
board will divide about $25,000 between
them. Attorney Frank Powers, represent
ing the merchant creditors, yesterday
filed with Auditor Wells a claim for that
amount, but the Auditor Bays there is not
that much in the fund, and that there i
are teachers as well as merchants whose |
claims are upon the fund, and that he ,
will act accordingly.

; Elrenfftb to sexual organs. Depot, â i Mark**GENERAL OTIS
NOT EQUAL TO
THE OCCASION
An Officer Says Many Lives and
Much Money Could Be Saved
in the Philippines.
An officer of one of the volunteer
regiments now in the Philippines
has written the following letter to
the Associated Press:
MANILA. June 27.â The arrival of the:
rainy season firi<ls the insurrection as
bulk. and vigorous as it has been any
time since the outbreak. The insurgent
armies arc well recruited, notwithstand
ing their heavy losses, and are well fed
and clothed. They have profited by their
five months of warfare against the Ameri
cans. They are fast adopting American
tactics ami are becoming better disci
plined and more skillful ln the use of
their weapons every day.
This is the unanimous judgment of the
troops who have been at the front all
through the fighting. The repeated stor
ies sent home about the collapse of the in
surrection are the fanciful dreams of cor
respondents and army officers, who never
venture outside the. walls of Manila.
The American people and the adminis
tration have been too long deceived with
regard to conditions here. Many lives and
millions of dollars will be saved by a
frank understanding of the true state of
affairs, followed by measures adequate to
crush th*- insurrection promptly. One hun
dred thousand soldiers should be here
ready for business by the beginning of the
dry season, in November. Garrisons could
then be stationed at strategic points and
freth troops be available to relieve 'n* s
exhausted columns. Continuous ; blows
would rapidly drive the Insurgents into
corners where I hey might be captured or
starved into submission, but a continuous
warfare cannot he carried on in this ener
vating climate by the same troops. 1- re
fluent reliefs are necessary. Troops should
not be kept here longer than a year. Men
from a northern climate retain their na
tive vigor for six or eight months after
arrival here, and then begin to succumb
to the. various ailments of tropical weath
er. This is exemplified in the case of the
volunteers and those regulars who have
been in the Philippines since last summer.
The most of them arc saturated with ma
laria, many have rheumatism and all are
greatly debilitated. They are unfit for
further duty. ami recuperation seems slow
and unsatisfactory. As it has been with
them so is it likely to be with their suc
cessors. _â -â ' , .
Bui the ope great need in a successful
campaign is a commanding general who
can keep the light going all along the
line at he same "time. It is a great prev
alent belief among the troops now in the
Philippines; regulars as well as volun
teers that General Otis is not equal to
the occasion. He is regarded as being al
together too one-sided, too cranky and too
Blow. Generals Lawton; Mac Arthur,
Wheato'n and Funston are favorites It
is an open secret in many circles that
Gener.ll Otis takes none, of them into his
confidence. Genarl Mac Arthur is the only
one of the four on friendly terms with
General Otis. ._ ,
A mistaken impression seems to obtain
in the United States as to the extent of
the insurrection. The 3,000,000 Tagals are
the leaders in it, but by no means are
thev'a'one. All reports from every island
in the archipelago agree that the Filipinos
everywhere are alike hostile to the Amer
icans although some carry their hostil
ity to greater lengths than others. All
alike have suffered from 300 years of
Spanish oppression and a like cause has
bred a like distrust of the white man. The
10,000,000 million people of the Philippines
are as highly civilized as the 12.000.000
people of Mexico. Their civilization, too.
lias many points of similarity to the Mex
ican, both having been derived from
Spanish sources.
If .j,, American people will Imagine, the
I'nited States to ha-.'- 1 acquired Mexico
agaih-t her will, am! to be engaged in an
attempt to put down a universal rebellion
of the Mexicans with 20,000 troops, they
will have a duplicate picture at close
range of the situation in the Philippines,
with the exception thai the climate of the
Philippines is from 10 to 15 degrees more
tropical than that of Mexico.
The volunteers here were much amused
at the news that the Postmaster General
had forbielder. the circulation of Edward
Atkinson's anti-expansionist documents
through the mails on the ground that
tlkv might create mutiny among the
troop= They accept this explanation as
evidence that the administration looks
upon the soldiers here is a band of chil
dren. The troops are virtually united
against the annexation of the Philippines,
regardless of Edward Atkinson and the
Postmaster-General.

ON AN OIL-TANK CAR
Southern Pacific Hospitality Illus- j
trated to the Disgust of
Sacramentans.
SACRAMENTO, July 81.â number of
mentans are making the air vocal
with complaints respecting the treatment j
they were subj< cted to last night at the j
hands of the Southern Pacific Company. i
A railroad trestle between Sacramento and j
Davis was discovered to be In flames, j
and when the fire had spent its rape fully
500 feet of the long structure had been
consumed. Long delays to trains result
ed. The local train from San Francisco and
two sections each of the Oregon express
and Eastern overland were dispatched to
Sacramento by way of Marysville. Some
of the local passengers for Sacramento
went by wav of Marysville. some walked,
and a number of them took a special train
; Which had been provided for them.
I This train, according to the statement of
Dr. (i c. Simmons, one of the complain-
I ing travelers, consisted of a locomotive
j and an oil-tank car. Men. women and
I children hung on to the car. the cow
-1 catcher, or found a place in the coal-laden
i tender. At 5 minutes before 2 o'clock this
| morning, four hours after the burning
1 trestle was discovered, the weary passen;
i gers were met a half-mile out from Sac
j ramento by an engine and coach.
' jhe delay is credited with having caused
! the passengers great discomfort, while it
i- claimed the ride on an oil car was an
â¢ unnecessary hardship. Blame is attached
to the officials at Oakland, the scene of
| the fire being not a part of the Sacra
i mento division.

LONG OBJECTS TO
PERSONAL COMMENTS
Advises Heads of Departments to
Mind Their Own
Affairs.
WASHINGTON, July 31. â Secretary
Long has taken notice of some rather
personal exchanges of opinion between
the Naval Bureaus In the course of official
business. Before he left the city to-day
he addressed an identical note to each of
the three bureausâ construction, engineer
ing and equipmentâ directing attention to
certain extracts from official letters that
were not proper, and warning the bureau
chiefs that comments similar to those
contained in the extracts were entirely
unnecessary and tended to retard the
transaction of business by provoking re
taliation. ". Â .*, ..
The bureaus are told to see to it that
in their communications hereafter all
such criticisms and expressions of judg
ment upon the way in which the duties
of other bureaus are performed and work
done are omitted. They are to confine
themselves to statements 'if opinion per
taining to the matter under considera
tion.

AROUND PUGET SOUND
Baby Presses the Button That Starts '

tric Plant.
SEATTLE. July 31.- Dorothy Elizabeth |

Charles H. Baker, president of the Sno- j
qualmie Falls Power Company, to-night
pressed the button that started the ma
chinery of one of the most powerful
plants in the Northwest and liberated j
3000 volts of electricity. This enterprise
was started by William T. Raker erf Chi
cago two years ago, and ,000,000 has
been expended to get the power of these
falls to market. The initial power is
thirty miles from Seattle, and it is so
thoroughly harnessed that all that will :
be needed" by Seattle. Tacoma and other
cities in this vicinity In many years, in
all kinds of industrial enterprises, will be j
available. .

! Demise Possibly Due to His Having
Stepped Upon a Rusty Nail.
PALO ALTO. July Henry R. Powell .
I of San Francisco died in Palo Alto on |
i Sunday. He was a member of the local
i firm of Powell & Dean, proprietors of the |
| Palo Alto planing mill and contractors
I and builders. He was a Knight of
| Pythias and past master of tne Ancient i
; Order of United Workmen. Mr. Powell
I was 31 years of age. For the past three
I weeks he had not been feeling well, and
! during this time he ran a nail into his
I foot. Inflammatory rheumatism set in, i
i and later typhoid fever, .to which his

! to San Francisco, and the funeral will be
I held mi Tuesday at 11 a. m. from the fam- ,
I tly residence at 3128 Sacramento street.

THE SA.N FHAISTCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 1, 1399.
CANDLER APPEALS
TO HIS PEOPLE
Wants Mob Rule Ended
in Georgia.
â â¦ â
INNOCENT OFTEN SUFFER
. â ' â
THE COTJUTS COMPETENT TO
PUNISH CRIMINALS.
Â»
Asks Lawyers to Abandon All Tech
nicalities and Pretexts Which
Tend to Defeat the Ends
of Justice.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ATLANTA, July 31.â Governor Candler
to-night issued an appeal to the people of
Georgia to, join hands and put an end to
mob violence in the State. The Governor !
believes the only way to restore a condi- I
tion of peace and tranquillity and to bring I
an end to. the lawlessness that has been
manifested in different parts of the State j
is for the people to uphold the courts, j
aid them in bringing to speedy "justice all j
criminals, and by the strong force of a
virile public sentiment bring punishment
to lawbreakers of all kinds. The Governor i
tells of the reproaches heaped upon the
State by the repeated outbreaks, and
says:
The purity of the fair mothers and daugh
ters nf Georgia must and shall be preserved,
and at the same time the lives and liberties I
of all the law-abiding: negroes in Georgia muj-t j
and shall be protected. Arson and burglary
anel assassination and robbery and m-sault
must stop, and at the same time lynch law
nice-* stop. The good of both races and the
fair name of the State demand this.
The ordinary processes of the law are amply
sufficient to punish all crimes. Our judges
are pure and incorruptible. Our juries are
composed of our most intelligent, upright men.
who seldom make mistakes. The men often
. makes mistakes and the innocent are made
!to suffer with the guilty. It never knows
whore to stop, but after punishing the guilty.
drunk with the blood of one victim, it thirsts
for the blood of another, and often sacrifice's
. on the altar of vengeance those wlu â  are
guiltless of any crime.
I We must away with the mob. We must f-
I enthrone the law. We must restore the altar
of reason and tear down the altar that pas
i sion has erected. We must do. this in th- in
; terest of the white men of Georgia and in the
Interest of the negroes of Georgia, and lor
I the fair name of Georgia, and to protect the
I virtue of the women of Georgia. Lynch law
I doer- not stop arson, nor murder, nor rob
-1 bery, no- assault. This requires the strong
, power of the statute law. sustained by a
healthy, vigorous public sentiment.
I would appeal to all officers of the State,
civil and military, urging them to remember
that the dignity and the fair name of Georgia
are in their keeping.
I would appeal to them to remember that
: they are the guardian of the peace and happi
i ness of the people of this State. It is their
duty to apprehend and bring to justice all
who violate the law. whether it he the negro
who commits assault or the white man who
i kills him for thÂ» crime. The -rani Juries
. must realize that it is as much their duty to
ferret out and return true bills against mem
bers of the mob who lynch a murderer as It
is to return a true bill against, the murderer
! himself.
I would appeal, especially to the liar, to bring
the potent Influence which the members of the
profession exercise in every community to the
] aid of law and order, not only by discounte
nancing mob rule, but to aid the courts and
I Juries in bringing the accused parties to speedy
i trial. It Is the duty of a lawyer to see that
; his client has a fair and impartial trial, hut
he should not resort to mere technicalities and
! pretexts to defeat the end of justice, or even
t'i delay the enforcement of penalties whereby
j society suffers and the confidence of the people
', in the ability of the courts to punish crime
I is destroyed.
In bringing about this end I would In
| yoke the active, earnest co-operation of all
! good men. white and black, with the officers
: of the law in their efforts to prevent crime,
suppress mob violence and bring criminals t.i
I Justice and to restore' peace and , order and
| tranquillity to all people of every race, class j
I and condition.

AUSTIN. July 31.â The extradition r.f
] John Keeton still hangs fire. Governor
i Savers to-day received the following let
ter from Judge A. A. Waltham. the United
States extradition agent at El Paso, Tex..
1 who has the matter in charge:
"I am in receipt of the additional pa
! pers sent me in the matter of the extra
, dition of John Keeton. now in jail in the
State of chihuahua. Mexico, and wanted
in the counties of Collin and Sutton for
! the crimes of murder and felonious theft.
"Immediately on receipt of the papers
; I made demand of the Consul here and
; went in person with him to Juarez to see
: the Federal Judge having the matter in
charge. They received the papers, but
, required translation of them into the
! Spanish language. I beg that you advise
me as to how. when and by whom the ex
pense of translation of the extradition pa
: pers and typewriter charges are to be
paid. I find nothing in the treaty which i
; would be construed to cover these cx
i penses. The papers are voluminous, and
much time is required to properly trans
late and prepare for submission.
"Vein will remember that the time of the
i commission of the two offenses by Keeton
I and Dale was prior to the time of the rat-
I ification of the present treaty with Mex
ico, and. as I understand it, it leaves the
matter wholly optional with the Mexican
Government as to whether a surrender
will be made. I learn that the Secretary
of State at Washington has recently de
clined to surrender a fugitive from Mex
ico on the ground that the crime ante
dated the treaty; and it may be. and in
my judgment likely will be, that unless
j a special effort is made through the Fed-
I eral channel the Mexican authorities will
take a similar view of the matter."
WAR OF THE MATCH
COMPANIES ENDED
Edwin Gould Admitted Into the Di
rectorate of the Diamond
Company.
CHICAGO. July Edwin Gould
of New Tork, for many years
considered the relentless and im
placable enemy of the Diamond Match
Company of Chicago, was admitted into
the directorate of that company by vote
of the stockholders at a special meeting
here to-day. Mr. Gould's admission to
the match board, following upon the ab
sorption of his match properties into the
Diamond Match Company, marks the final
and formal ending of the -war." which
has. bitterly vexed and troubled stockhold
ers in the Diamond Match Company.
James Hopkins of St. Louis was also
elected to be a new director of the com
pany, his election and Gould's being to
till the two new places provided for by the
vote passed by the stockholders increas
ing the number of directors from five to
seven. Mr. Hopkins was one of the orig
inal large stockholders of the Diamond
Match Company, and until three years
ago was also a director of the company.
It is said that Mr. Gould will be given
nearly $1,000,000 In .Diamond Match stock
for his interest in the Continental Match
Company.
Besides the election of two new direct
ors the Diamond, Match stockholders
oassed a resolution increasing the capital
stock from $11,000,000 to $15,000,000, to take
effect September 15. 'â¢'..:_::

Boring for Artesian Water.
WOODLAND, July 31.â 1t has long been
the contention of men of experience and
much observation that an abundant flow
of artesian water may be obtained in
Capay Valley by boring to a sufficient
depth. J. -W. Shannon of San Jose owns
a fine orchard near Guinda. He recently
began to bore a well on his premises.
Citizens of Guinda and the surrounding
country decided to experiment and in
duced 'Mr. Shannon to bore deeper than
at first intended, on condition that he be
paid so much a foot after going below the
200-foot level. If artesian water is found
Mr. Shannon is to refund all subscrip
tions. If the. experiment is a failure none
of the subscribers are to have any claim
against Mr. Shannon. The experiment will
be watched with a great deal of interest.

ROCHESTER, N. V.. ' July 31.â
After a vexatious day the trans
continental automobile reached
Rochester at 9:10 o'clock to
night, coming down Main street
lat eighteen miles an hour. Newark
! was left at 8:15 this morning and thÂ«
i run made to Palmyra. The new nuts
on the inlet valves persisted in loosen
ing and falling off. A stop was made
at Palmyra to renew the supply of
| nuts and the trip resumed at 3:30
j o'clock with a new device for holding
the nuts in place. This worked beauti
fully until at Pittsford, when the spin
dle in the rear valve broke, taking the
nuts and causing a delay of two hours
while temporary repairs were made.
The run of eight miles into Rochester
was made in thirty minutes.
The inlet valves will have to he made
still heavier, and we are having the
spindles made five-sixteenths inches in-
' stead of quarter-rind*., as at present.
Everything else is entirely satisfactory
and we will start for Buffalo about 9
I o'clock. The roads are bettor than in
' the East, though on both sides of Fair
port we had heavy roads of sand six
inches or deeper. This and the grades
made the motor work hard, but it was
equal to every call and carried us over
the. worst of the day's run safely. We
had several rickety bridges to cross and
some steep grades. .

TWO MEET DEATH IN
THE ELECTRIC CHAIR
Louis Pullerson and Michael McDon
ald Executed in Sing Sing
â¢ fc- Prison. . ,
NEW YORK, July 31.â Louis Pullerson
and Michael McDonald were put to death
by electricity at Sing Sing prison to-day.
Pullerson was taken to the electric chair
at 8-21 o'clock and a current 1720 volts
strong was turned on at 5:22. After fifty
five seconds he was declared to be dead
by the attending physicians.
'McDonald was put to death at 8:42
o'clock, a current of 1710 volts being
turned on at that time and continuing for
sixty-five seconds. McDonald's body re
sisted the electric current more than any
i other man put to death in Sing Sing. It
took ten seconds longer to kill him than
it did Pullerson.
Louis Pullerson. a negro porter, 20 years
old. on March 11. 1898, killed his common
law wife. Kate Smith, a white woman, in
their apartments in New York by stran
l gulation. He said that as the woman had
I been untrue he thought he had a perfect
right to kill her. "
Michael McDonald, a beef carrier, em
ployed in the Eastern Company's slaugh
ter-house in New York, on May 4. IS!'**,
killed Stephen Titus, the head timekeeper.
McDonald, after spending his salary in
drink, demanded several dollars which he
claimed were due him for overtime. Titus
told him to have the sub-timekeeper make
out the bill. McDonald then fired several
shots at Titus.
Anti-Saloonists Victorious.
PALO ALTO. July 31.â The petition of
Nils Johnson to reopen the Little Ven
dome saloon at Mayfleld has been with
drawn from the . Board of Supervisors.
When the petition was first presented
there was much opposition to it, which
culminated in a vigorous protest to the
Supervisors against its being granted.
The protest was signed by President Da
vid Starr Jordan and other members of
the Stanford faculty, and by many of the
property owners of Mayfleld. They ob
jected to' the saloon on account or the
bad influence of the place on some of the
students, and the withdrawal of the peti
tion is virtually a victory for -the forces
which had united against the saloon.
