A REPUBLICAN JUBILEE.
Harbor Club Extends Greeting to j
Congressman Kahn.
The Harbor Republican Club held a
general jollification meeting last night at
its rooms at Davis and Washington
streets. incidental to the refreshments j
and general good time there was some j
excellent speech-making. Congressman
Kahn, Henry B. Maddison. the- club's
president; and other eloquent speakers
being sent. . .
Congressman Kahn said that his first ;
efforts at legislation would be for the
enactment of laws thai would work the
rehabilitation of the merchant marine
and 'hat he did hot think it would be
many years before ships flying the Amer- |
lean flag would be carrying the commerce
of all parts of the world. "This has been
a railroad-building country for years;
now it is about i" become a shipbuilding
nation." said Kahn.
Among the speakers were Henry B. j
Maddison. G. G. Camasa of the Italian-
Swiss Colony and Captain Charles Pratt. I

HARMONY DID NOT PREVAIL.
Consolidated Democratic Clubs in the ,
Fortieth District at War.
All was not harmony in the Fortieth
District Democratic Club last night at
Bear Hall. A month ago the club pre
sided over by Edward R. Rock md the
one presided over by Bernard Bienenfield
were consolidated and the latter was.
made president of the new organization.
The trouble last night occurred over the
fact that the Rock faction was only ;
granted eleven delegates to the conven
tion, while the Bienenfield club exacted
fourteen. 77 ,_. .. ... J
Sidney Van Wyck objected to the hold- .
in"- of the election of delegates, claiming
that Rock had the rolls of both clubs in j
his possession and refused to allow the
committee on enrollment to examine and
certify to their correctness. After an ac
rimonious debate a motion to adjourn sine
die was carried and a number of the mem
bers filed out of the hall, and they say
they will meet next Wednesday to elect
heir own delegates.

The Forty-third Assembly District Re

Deckhands on River Steamers
Strike.
! There was another strike on the Sacra- J
I mento River steamers yesterday. Last |
| week the freight handlers, struck for a
' raise from 30 cents to 50 cents an hour.
j After a conference with the agents of the j
! vessels a compromise at 40 cents an hour
I was reached, and the men went back to ;
i work. Festerday the deckhands on the
I steamers Relief and Onward went on i
j strike. They have been getting $40 a
month, and how that the busy season Is
!on they want $50. Another conference ,
j was held and the owners offered $15 aj
month, but the men refused point-blank to ;
| accept it. and walked off the steamers.
After considerable delay a new crew
j from among the men on the wharf was
i secured for the Onward, and she started
away for Sacramento River points about
11 a. m. A new crew for the Relief was
not cured until the afternoon, and it ;
was 5 p. m. before she got away.
NORMAL SCHOOL FACULTY.
Nominations by Dr. Burk Confirmed
by the Trustees.
The trustees of the San Francisco Nor
mal School met last night in Secretary
Dinkelspiel's office in the Claus Spreckeis
building. Judge S. C. Denson presided.
Dr. Fred E. Burk nominated the follow
ing teachers to constitute the faculty, all
of which were confirmed by the board:
leorge L. Leslie, Miss Agnes Stowell,
Professor Elmer E. Brown, Professor Ell
wood Cubberley and Miss Estelle Carpen
ter. .
Mr. Leslie is a graduate of the Universi
ties of lowa and Chicago, and is at pres
ent head of the science denartment of the
Los Angeles High School and first vice
president of the Southern California Acad
emy of Sciences. Miss Stowell is a gradu
ate of the State Normal School of San
Jose, and has taken a course in Stanford
and Chicago universities In the depart
ment of education. She has occupied var
ious responsible educational positions, and
Is the assistant editor of the Oregon
Teachers' Monthly. Mr. Leslie and Miss
Stowell have not been assigned to any
particular brancti as yet.
Professor Brown is the head of the ped
agogical department at Berkeley, and
Professor Cubberley occupies a similar
position at Stanford. They will deliver a
lourse of lectures during the school term.
Miss Carpenter will be in charge of the
singing department, and is now superin
tendent of singing in the public schools.
Mrs. Mary Hardwigson was appointed
janitress of the Normal School.
It was decided to limit the number of
pupils to 100 for the present.
The following resolutions were adopted,
providing for the establishment of a
model school in connection with the Nor
mal School:
Resolved. That the president of the school
he authorized to establish model classes In
connection with the Normal School, a.l
mitting pupils of the regular primary and
grammar grades: that the Instruction shall
be given by the members of the Normal
School faculty, assist.-. 1. when practicable,
i, v the students of the school, the* aim
being to illustrate the best educational
methods of teaching and hygienic provisions
of development: that so far as possible the
course of study pursued in the public
schools be followed, so that pupils may be
received from and transferred to the cor
responding grades of the public schools;
providing that the total number of pupils
admitted to the model school shall not ex
ceed 120; that' the number in any one class
shall not exceed fifteen, and that a tuition
fee of '_ per month, payable quarterly In
advance, be required.

OSGOOD THE BIGAMIST.
George L. Osgood, the whisky drummer.
â¢was arraign* I before Judge Mogan yes
terday afternoon on the second charge
of bigamy against him. In which the com
plaining witness is Richard Bourne, 32
Second street, and his preliminary exam
ination was set for to-morrow along with
the first case.
Osgood's third wife is living in this city
and the police are busily engaged try
ing to locate her. He has also another
wife in Texas. It is said that one of his
wives has engaged Attorney Peter Dunne
to defend him.
Osgood's past record Is being exposed
gradually, It was discovered yesterday
that about four years ago he. was charged
in Judge- Conlan court with forgery and
obtaining money by false pretenses and
the cases were dismissed through a mis
take of the complaining witness in not
appearing in court on the- day set for his
preliminary examination.

â¢â¢Barrel" Watts Is No More.
William Watts, better known as "Bar
rel" Watts, a member of the notorious
Watts family, died early this morning at
the County Jail of pneumonia. Watts re
cently served a term for petty larceny
and was awaiting a trial in the Superior
Court for grand larceny when his death
occurred. The last crime he committed
was the theft of a horse from Contractor
Young, and at the time of his arrest he
was chased eighteen blocks by Officer
Irvine before capture. He was a notori
ous criminal and had served many terms
in the penitentiary. He derived the ap
pellation of "Barrel" for having rolled a
small boy down North Beach in a barrel.

Two sensational complaints foreboding

simultaneously a lis pendens was recorded
'â  against all of the property of the Bacon
Land and Loan Company, located in San
Joaquin, El Dorado and Alameda counties,
by Professor Frank Soul., and Mamie C.
â  Bacon, formerly the wife of Frank P.
Bacon.

SPICY ANSWER IN THE
SMITH DIVORCE CASE
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, July 31.
Norman H. Smith of Wisconsin, through
his attorney, H. S. Aldrich. to-day filed
a sensational answer and cross-complaint
in the divorce suit commenced on June 15
last by his wife, Minnie Smith, who
gained considerable notoriety last month
when she charged aged Arthur Arlington,
the bunkoer of widows, with having
robbed her of $215.
Arlington was held on June 14 to answer
before the Superior Court, and on the fol
lowing day Mrs. Smith tiled a divorce suit
against her husband, charging extreme
cruelty, Her original complaint recites
that she and Norman Smith were married
at West Superior, Wis., in May, 1!<93, and
that for a year prior to bringing this ac
tion she had been a resident of Califor
nia. The acts of cruelty narrated extend
from Mabton, Wis., to the State of Wash
ington and to San Diego. The wife al
leges that on one occasion her husband
tore her clothes, threw a pistol at her and
drove her from their home at Mabton with
her infant; that he frequently killed the
chickens and destroyed the eggs so that
she could have nothing to eat; that when
she went to live with her sister in L- is
Angeles in 1885, defendant husband fol
lowed her and made life with him unbear
able' that while living with him at Santa
Monica he choked her and threatened to
shoot her, as well as her sister, and.
finally that on one occasion tho husband
spoiled a Christmas dinner by pouring
kerosene over it. . â ,A .^
The husband threatens to fight the ease
bitterly. He has telegraphed his attorney
that he is now on his way to this State,
and he promises to make matters decid
edly interesting. It appears that he him
self sued for a divorce last February, and
in answering said suit Mrs. Smith admit
ted that she was a resident of Wisconsin
for more than a year next preceding tne
commencement of the suit. Now in her
complaint she swears she has a residence
In California. c , â . . 7. . . ,
In his answer filed to-day the husband,
after denying the allegations of cruelty
and his wife's residence in this State,
makes grave charges of her having disre
garded the solemnity of her marriage
vows on November 24, 1898, and January.
1889 at Lake Mills. Wis., naming August
H Wegeman as co-respondent, and on
May 14 1890. ; " San Francisco, infidelity
is again charged, this time A. Arlington
being named as co-respondent.
Smith does not ask for a decree of di
vorce but prays that his wife take noth
ing by her suit and that he be given cus
tody "of their minor child. Jessie Smith,
alleging that the mother is not a fit and
proper person to have control of her.

NATIONAL PLANS
FOB WASHINGTON
STREET BRIDGE
Solution of Difficulty
Is Proposed.
â â¦ â
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, July 31.
The trouble over the Washington-street
bridge is now in a fair way to disappear.
The situation of affairs at present .points
to an abandonment of Washington avenue
as a thoroughfare in the Fruitvale dis
trict.
J. R. Talcott, chairman of Supervisors,
sent the following letter to W. H. Heuer.
major. Corps of Engineers, United States
army:
OAKLAND. July 24. 1899.â W.H.. H. Heuer, Esq..
major. Corps of Engineers. U. S. A.â Dear Sir:
In yours of July 21 to the Board of Supervisors
of Alameda County you say "As I cannot build
two combined bridges starting from a common
point etc.," and asking us to waive the con
struction of any bridge for highway traffic
across canal, other than that proposed at
Fruitvale avenue, between Park and High
streets, I would say:
We take it for granted from the reading of
the above that the railroad company will not
require a bridge on Washington avenue and
that they also have been requested to waive
their right of way on said Washington avenue.
Are we correct? Respectfully yours,
J. R. TALCOTT, Chairman Supervisors.
The reason for the delay, according to
Major Heuer's reply, which is quite
lengthy depends upon the necessity of
things as it is compulsory to build the
bridge before the tidal canal can be com
pleted. The most important section of
the letter Is as follows:
The railroad company expressed a willing
ness to waive the construction of a bridge
at Washington avenue provided the Govern
ment would furnish a right of way, and lay
tracks on the same, connecting by a short
practicable route the proposed Fruitvale ave
nue bridge with existing Fern_ride-M.el.-ose
track. This appeared fair and equitable, an.l
if It could have been accomplished would have
saved the Government an expense of at least
$30,000. lt was ascertained, however, that there
was no law which would permit of the ac
ceptance of such terms, and such solution
could not be further considered.
The railroad company could not be expected
to purchase the strip of land above referred to
and lav track on same at an expense of 110.
to (13,000 without some compensating advan
tage, and in the absence of law the Govern
ment could not transfer to the railroad com
pany a strip of land and connecting track,
even though all the advantage*, were on the
side of the Government. The exchange, if lt
could be so called, would require special action
by Congress, and If authorized would require
that the land necessary for the connection be
acquired by condemnation proceedings. Judg
lag from what has occurred In the past in
similar proceedings of this work, this would
have delayed the completion of the tidal canal
for at least six years, to the detriment of the
work Itself and to the prejudice of the peo
ple of Alameda County.
The matter now rests with you. As yet I
have nee legal waiver of your right to a high
way bridge at Washington avenue. I am
looking solely to the rapid completion of the
won. as authorized by Congress. If you waive
thÂ» right to a highway bridge at Washington
avenue unconditionally. I believe the work of
completing Oakland harbor can go on uninter
ruptedly, If you cannot see your way clear
to do this, the work must cease as soon as I
have made contracts for and completed High
street and Fruitvale avenue bridges, and be
delayed until Congress modifies its previous
action, furnishes funds and authorizes a
change In the existing project. Very respect
fully W. H. HEUER.
Major, Corps of Engineers, '*. S. A.
Crushed Between Gravel Cars.
ALAMEDA, July 31.â John- May, who
resides on Encinal avenue, near High
street, was injured Friday night at the
"sink." He was engaged in unloading
gra el from dumpcars, when the train
started. In trying to save himself from
falling under the wheels May got his left
arm caught between the bumpers. Both
bones of the forearm were splintered, .ie
sharp ends protruding through the fl;sh.
Crushed by Ealling Pipe.
BERKELEY. July 31.â Barrett Fisher,
an employe of the Hofburg Brewery in
West Berkeley, is In a critical condition
at the East Bay Sanitarium as the result
of attempting to move some of the fix
tures of the brewery. While handling
some barrels a heavy iron pipe fell from
an upper story and struck th- man on
the head, severely crushing his face and
neck. While the injuries did not prove
fatal, their extent Is such that Fisher
may be made an invalid for life.

Otis Nelson and Harry Stewart went to
the residence of Chris 'Christiansen, on
Tenth avenue, near Q street. South San
Francisco, last night, and in a scuffle
which followed Nelson fell and broke his

Hospital and attended by Dr. Lane. A

Stewart, and < 'hristianson will swear out
a warrant for the arrest of Nelson to-day.
Christianson claims that the two men.
who were drunk, came to his house and
proceeded to assault him without any
provocation. Nelson is a laborer, 28 years
old, and lives on Fifth avenue, near M
street.

THREATENS TO
POISON A BABY
Being Dismissd He
Seeks Revenge.
â â¢ â
ALAMEDA, July Mrs. George R.
Chambers of 1829 Clinton avenue has re
ceived anonymous threats through the
mail that unless she dismisses her Japa
nese servant he will be shot and the milk
used by her baby will be poisoned. The
threatening missive bears the Oakland
postmark and is supposed to be the work
of a Chinaman named Kay, or a Japanese
who covets the position now held by Mrs.
Chambers' servant.
Ah Kay worked in the Chambers house
hold up to last Wednesday, when he was
summarily dismissed. He was indignant
at being discharged and muttered some
thing to the effect that Mrs. Chambers
would be sorry. The following Friday
Mrs. Chambers received the annexed let
ter:
Mrs. Chamber: Me yon China frien. Me
no iik sen my fren go. Kay. No Ilk opostte
house .Trippâ-, make you my fren go. You
see him. I kill him, poison baby milk every
lavs. Me no care other girl other Jappee.
HI BIN DAS LEE.
Mrs. Chambers took the letter to the
Chinese cook at the home of Mrs. Peter
White, her aunt, who had recommended
Kay. He insisted that Kay had no knowl
edge of English, and that as far as high
binders were concerned he claimed that
he was "boss" of one of the tongs and
he would take it upon himself to see that
no harm was done the Chambers family
from that source.
Mr. Chambers, who is secretary of Glad
ding. McÃean & Co. of San Francisco,
was not at home when the letter was re
ceived and did not arrive until Saturday.
He at once reported the matter to the po
lice and every precaution has been taken
to prevent "Hibandas Lee" from putting
his threat into effect.
PRIVILEGES ABUSED.
Well-to-Do Residents of Alameda
Insist Upon Free Vaccination.
ALAMEDA, July 31.â Health Officer L.
W. Stidham is authority for the assertion
; that the well-to-do people and society
! lights of Alameda have been systemati
cally abusing the free vaccination privi
'- leges. The Board of Health intended the
privilege for those who were financially
' unable to have their children comply with
the school restrictions requiring vaccina
tion. Blanks were printed for the signa
tures of the applicants setting forth that
I the parent was unable to pay a physi
cian. ;
Dr. Stidham says that some of the so
' ciety leaders of the city have applied for
these blinks. signed them without
, compunction and sent their children with
them to the Health Office. Ladies dre.s-.ed
in silks and bedecked with diamonds have
applied to Dr. Stidham to vaccinate their
children free of charge, asserting without
a blush that they were too poor to pay
' for the operation.
The Board of Health will take action to
prevent the abuse of the free vaccination
. privilege in future.
DR. MONTAGUE ARRIVES.
BERKELEY, July .11.â William P.
Montague of Harvard University, who
has been elected as instructor in the de
partment of philosophy in the University
of California, has arrived from the East,
and is making preparations for the work
of the coming term.
Dr. William Montague is a Harvard
man, and he is leaving the big Eastern
university to come to the Pacific Coast.
For some years he has been an instructor
under Professor William James, the psy
chologist, who visited Berkeley last year.
He is regarded by Professor James as
one of the most promising scholars among
the younger men in philosophy.
Dr. Montague was chosen by the Board
of Regents to succeed Dr. E. B. McC.il
varv. who has recently gone to Cornell
University to take charge of the chair of
philosophy there. During the absence of
Professor Harrison in Europe. Dr. Mon
tague will occupy his residence on Ban
croft way.

Fraternity Robbed.
BERKELEY. July 31.â The Phi Delta
Theta. a fraternity on the corner of Du
rant avenue and Dana street, has suf
fered another loss at the hands of burg
lars. The fraternity recently lost its
chapter house by tire, and during the re
building all the furniture and personal
effects of the college men have been
stored in a barn behind the house. Two
months ago the barn was entered by a
gang of boys and completely rifled of all
the valuables removable. The goods were
recovered and the boys let off with a
caution. The caution seems to have had
no effect, however, as the same boys are
said to have stolen the identical things
again. Marshal Lloyd Is on the lookout
for the perpetrators.
A peculiar and important difference be
tween petroleum as used In Europe and
In the United States is that in the latter
the crude article is employed as it comes
from the well, while Russia and Italy
an.l other European countries use tho
refuse that is left after the lighter con
stituents of the oil have been removed by
a partial distillation. . â -,'â ?â .

PERRAULT AND
PHELAN DISCUS.
THE TAX LEVY
Corkscrew Franchise
Is Granted.
â â
FUNDS FOR THE HOSPITALS
â * â
COUNTY INSTITUTION â TO BE
IMPROVED.
â¦-
Supervisors Argue Different Phases
of Appropriations. That Must Be
Made, but Decide to Wait
Upon the Merchants.
Supervisor Perrault precipitated some
rather heated debate anent the tax levy
at the board meeting yesterday by calling
upon Mayor Phelan to say why he had not
kept his word to the Finance Committee
by submitting an official statement show
ing how the numerous departments might J
be provided for In the levy and the dollar
limit maintained.
Mayor Phelan denied he had ever prom
ised to outline a plan, but said he had de
clared himself ready at any time to meet
the committee and render it advice and
assistance. Supervisor Perrault insisted
that his Honor had promised an official
statement, and his Honor Anally ceased
to deny It and avoided further distasteful
debate by bringing up the dire need of
the Health Department in so -far as the
Receiving Hospital was concerned. He \
said he had been informed by the depart- ;
ment that $500 to be expended in sanitary i
plumbing would be needed before the â¢â 
hospital could be reopened, and he handed
down a resolution allowing it $175. Super- !
visor Collins made it an occasion to
strongly criticize the action of the health
authorities in' closing the establishment,
ami said in support of his allegations thai
he had been in the hospital several times
in the past seven months and had never ,
noticed the odor of sewer gas or anything
else objectionable. Despite Mr. Collins'
argument the resolution was adapted and
the hospital will be open again for busi
ness as soon as the necessary sewer con
nections can be made.
Supervisor Phelps stirred up more dis
cussion by asking who had let the con
tract for elevators in the new Hall of
Justice. His attention was called to the
matter by a resolution before the board
to pay th- contractors $13,300 for building
them. No one knew who had let the
contract, and Supervisor Holland, chair
man of the Building Committee, declared
that the claim had never been allowed
by his committee and was not properly
before the board. Further discussion de
veloped the fact that the original con
trait called, for five elevators to cost not
more than 114.000, but that only four ele
vators had been built, and $13,300 was de
manded for them. It also transpired that
the contract had been let by Shea & Shea,
the architects, without authority by reso
lution of the board. It proved too knotty
a problem to handle at one sitting and
was Anally referred to the Building Com
mittee for investigation.
Supervisors Lackmann and Perrault of
fered a resolution requesting Governor
Gage to declare the day of the arrival of
the Fust California In this city a legal
holiday. It was adopted unanimously, as
was another, extending a cordial invita
tion to President ParArio Diaz of Mexico
to visit this city when he makes his tour
of this country In September and Octo- 1
ber next. . _
Hi.is for the "corkscrew franchise
asked for by the Market Street Railway
system were opened, and that company
proved to be the only bidder. Its pro
posal was made on the lines laid down in
' the charter and was accepted by unani
mous vote of tin- board. The board also
adopted the plan proposed by the Mer
chants' Association for the purchase of
Fire Department supplies. On motion of
Byington, it was amended so as to in
clude every department of the city gov
ernment.
The statement of the Market-street
i Railway Company that the gross income
from certain of the lines of its system
; during the past fiscal year was $48,-),
was approved, and $0715 __â 2 per cent of
the receiptsâ was ordered paid Into the
treasury. This payment will make pay
able the Street Department labor claims
for the month of June. ;
A resolution was adopted rescinding
I that portion of the temporary tax levy
i which appropriated $600 for the payment
I of the clerk of the Board of Equalization.
The amount was Axed at $1200 instead,
and the clerks, who have been unpaid,
will now receive their money. .-''-';
A resolution directing the Finance Com
mittee to make a special appropriation in
the tax levy for the sanitary improvement
of the City and County Hospital was re
i ferred to the Building Committee to de
i termine what the necessary improvements
' would cost. _ _, _
City and County Attorney Lane tiled an
opinion with the board which establishes
the contention that the grade of a street
once fixed cannot be changed, except un
der a general adjustment of damages. The
property directly under consideration was
a lot at' Twenty-first and Sanchez streets.
The grade of the street at that place was
Axed in 1866 at 340 feel above the base. In
1888 the board raised it fifteen feet, render
ing property along the line practically
valueless. Owners petitioned the board to
repeal the resolution of 'SS, but Mr.
Lane decided that the matter was already
settled In their favor In numerous de
cisions of the Supreme Court.
Lackmann moved to adjourn at the call
of the chair that the board might meet
with the Merchants' Association when
that body brings in its tax levy, and after
some discussion his motion was permitted
to prevail. â¢ ... -.:â ".â¢â¢

BOSS BUCKLEY DISCLOSES
UGLY SECRETS OF THE PAST
The Examiner Was Sold Out More Than Once to
Huntingtonâ lnside Story of the Mayne
Railroad Franchise Scheme.
Buckley Reveals Interesting Incidents in His Career as Confidential
Adviser and Political Manager of the Examiner,
SPECTATORS of the fight between
Buckley on one side and the Rai
ney-Examiner league on the other
expected thai the Rainey newspa
per which made, a few days ago.
such a furious attack on Boss Buckley
would follow up the assault! As Buck
ley promptly responded with ugly dis
closures concerning th Examiner's as
sociation with bosses, the unterrifled
Democrats, who enjoy a fight to the l
finish, suppnsed that the Examiner j
would forthwith turn loose on the i
"Blind White Devil" and do some rare
roasting. The surprise of the campaign j
is the silence of the Examiner. The j
Democrats are wondering If the Ral- i
Examiner league has not taken
alarm at the prospect of a controversy
which may disclose to the public the
long-concealed truth about the pur
chase of a seat in the United States
Senate.
Good citizens rejoiced at the prospect
of a fight between old-time busses and
political manipulators, believing in the â¢
.'..lac,- that "honest men get their dues j
when rogues fall out."
The fact is well established that the
relations which existed between the
Examiner and Buckley for many years
were confidential and intimate. The old
Evening Examiner was purchased at
the suggestion of Buckley and con- j
verted into a morning light. "When I
there was no money at the business j
office to pay the hands the conductors j
of the paper went to Buckley's Bush- .
street saloon and borrowed the coin j
to meet the deficit. When doubts arose i
in the editorial department as to what
course the paper should pursue on na
tional. State nd local affairs Buckley
was summoned. A carriage was sent,
to bring him post haste to the office. j
Time, however, changes all things, j
Buckley Is no longer the source of Ex- ;
aminer inspiration, A new boss has i
arisen and th" St. George Stable is the
fountain of Democratic wisdom am! re- j
form.
iking of the past. Buckley, who
was yesterday in a serene and remi- I
niscent frame if mind, contributed the j
following chapter to the political an
nals of San Francisco:
"When young Hearst obtained pos
session of the paper my advice was i
often sought by him. Frequently a car
riage would be sent to my house and I ;
would go to the Examiner office and
there be interrogated by its proprietor
ns to the policy of the paper on mat
ters of political import. 1 also ap- |
peared in the capacity of a friend and
attorney of the paper to settle a libel.
suit that might have cost the paper
thousands of dollars and possibly sent I
Its editor to State's prison." '
"What case was that?" he was asked, |
"That was the case of Hon. F. F. j
Strother," he replied.
"What were the circumstances?" !
' Mr. Strother was the Auditor of the I
city," continued Buckley, "and to my !
knowledge proved the best we ever had. i
Well, he had nerve enough to give the
Bulletin or some other paper some !
printing and as this was antagonistic j
to the-. Examiner it attacked him un- j
mercifully. They called him a male- \
factor and other strong names and Mr.
Strother promptly secured the services
of S. M. Wilson and commenced a suit
against the paper for a very large sum
pf money for damages. The Examiner
conductors sent for me to help them
cut of the affair and it was only after
a hard struggle that we could get Mr.
Strother to withdraw his suit, as we
thought it v. mid be detrimental to the
Democratic party. He did not, how
ever, dismiss the suit until thÂ« Ex
aminer had made public reparation.
"If I am the bad man I am accused j
of being; if I am the character they are I
presenting to the public, why is it they j
Bought my advice for their paper; why j
is it they asked my aid in suits of j
libel: why did the proprietor of that !
sheet ask me to take charge of his
fight for Governor; to get the delega
tion of this city to support him for the ;
Democratic nomination and also later ;
to have him appointed and elected
United States Senator?
"It was during the period, from 1880
to IS9O that the organization of which >
I was a member was a grand one. I
was all right then. I was continually
doing these favors for Mr. Hearst In :
1890, when 1 refused to do what I con
sidered dishonorable and since which
time I have been out of politics, I be- I
came this very bad man. During that j
time, from 1882 to 1891, when, according
to that paper I was in control of the !
Democratic organization, this city was
better governed than it was before or
has been since. If I was the bad man
they represent me to be why did they
come to me time and again and accept
my money to keep their paper going; I
why did they pet me to settle their libel
suitâ help get its proprietor the :
nomination for Governor?
"They say I am in the employ of j
Huntington. That's a lie! I am not in i
the employ of Mr. Huntington and have |
never been. But it Is well known to
every resident of this community that
a short time ago the Examiner was in
the employ of Mr. Huntington, under a
contract of one thousand dollars a
month for thirty months. And I want j
to say right here that that is not the
only time to my knowledge that the j
Examiner sold out to Mr. Huntington.
"In that article they say I was fined
$."00 in a contempt proceding before
the Supreme Court. That is another
malicious lie. The record shows that a
majority of that court saw through the
conspiracy and exonerated me by dis
missing the proceeding.
"Now as to this Wallace Grand Jury.
That is the great conspiracy they are
in the habit 'if Haunting every few
day to the public. In connection with
that matter I want to state that Wil
liam T. Wallace asked me to have him
nominated In the Democratic conven- j
Hon for Superior Judge, which I did.
and he was elected. At the San Jose
convention in 1890 he was a candidate
for the nomination of Chief Justice of
ji
the Supreme Court. He was defeated
in the convention and he attributed his
defeat to me and he swore in the pres
ence of his colleagues on the bench that
he would be revenged on me. In IS9I
he formed what was known as the
Wallace Grand Jury, which was in op
position to the law he had laid down
while Justice of the Supreme Court "of
this State, and which we all know was
I set aside by that tribunal.
"That so-called Grand Jury found
' what they called an indictment against
I me for something I had no more to do
j with than the man in the moon had.
; A man by the name of Mayne tried to
i interest me in a scheme to obtain a
railroad franchise from the Board of
Supervisors, and I refused to have any
thing to do with it. His attorney, who is
now a resident of this city, also asked
me to interest myelf and I gave him the
very same answer. I also advised my
friends on the Board of Supervisors
j to have nothing to do with it. In No-
I vember, 1890, I left for Europe. In
i December of that same year or just be-
I fore the expiration of the term of that
j Board of Supervisors, and at a time
j when 1 was seven thousand milesaway,
PRIZES AWARDED TO
SUCCESSFUL TURNERS
THE seventeenth biennial fes
tival of the Pacific Turn Bezirk
came to a close at an early
hour this morning at Turner
Hall, with the last strains of
"Home, Sweet Home." The last,
flay was a long one. crowded with
events from the assembly at 7 o'clock
yesterday morning till the last step in
the last dance on the programme began
last evening, but not a Turner in the
district and not one of his friends
who begrudged one of the many
hours spent.
The weather conditions were much
better than on the preceding day and
the sixty odd athletes entered in the
various events went at their work
with a will. In the amphitheater the
men continued their feats of the day
before on the horizontal bar. They
jumped, high and broad, they went
hand over hand tip the rope and they
sprinted, In the barn they finished
before noon on the apparatus.
Popular interest in the festival
reached its highest pitch during the
afternoon, when the wrestlers went
on the mat in "the Barn." Eight
athletes representing the Eintracht
and San Francisco vereins of this city
and the Oakland and Sacramento
vereins were entered to contest for
the- laurel wreath and diploma that
constituted the first and second prizes.
Before the bouts began the Impres
sion had gained ground that William
Oechsle of the Verein Eintracht would
down all comers. The Sacramento
contingent, however, which was out
for blood, was confident it had a r^ni
in William Noething who would give
the Eintracht's representative a tussle
for the place. As after events proved
the Sacramentans were wiser than
they knew.
The first bout was a short, sharp
Struggle between Fred Ottinger of the
San Francisco Turn Verein and Her
man Haustein of the Eintracht. Hau
stein won by very clever work.
Oechsle, the champion of the Ein
tracht, then appeared to try conclu
sions with Louis Trost of tu< San
Franclscos. His appearance was the
signal for prolonged cheering. The
Eintracht wrestler showed a larger
amount of muscle than his opponent,
and went at his work in a way to show
that he was a practiced hand. He was
in no hurry to throw his man, but he
showed a confidence and a skill that
were too much for Trost, who soon
went on his shoulders.
The Sacramento dark horse, Noeth
ing, was pitted against Ernest Rehor
of the Oakland Verein. He heightened
the hopes of his admirers by his work
manlike manner of going at his oppo
nent, and soon put him hors de combat
with a half Nelson.
The succeeding bout between Joe
Myers and Arthur Hluniel of the San
Francisco Turn Verein was a clever
exhibition in a class all by itself, Blu
mel finally losing to his opponent, who
threw him once more for luck. After
a slight Intermission the champions
came together to determine the ques-

i.i i tit before the crowd cooled down

three years ago, which is fresh in the
minds of the people? They remember

levy, according to the newspapers, and
their, ideas were that a levy of $2 50 on

could run the government with. Mayor
Phelan turned them out of office and
recommended a reputable lot of gentle
men in their stead, who while in office
prepared a tax levy of $1 25 on a $100
valuation, which was copied by these
fellows at the meeting they held in the
corridors, of the City Hall, and which
officially became the tax levy, thereby
saving to the people an enormous sum
of money.
"Now if Democrats want to return
to this sort of government let them
vote for the delegate ticket submitted
to them by the Examiner and its un
holy alliance. The Examiner has been
villifying and blackguarding every per
son who comes in these headquarters
in a language known only to the in
mates of the hovel in which it is ground
out."
Sudden Death of Mrs. Duckworth.
Mrs. Lillian E. Duckworth died suddenly
last Sunday of heart failure at the resi
dence of her sister, Mrs. Brown, 405 Va
lencia street. Mrs. Duckworth was the
daughter of Charles Underwood, a promi
nent citizen of Monterey. Her husband,
B. E. Duckworth, is a brother of S. J.
Duckworth, former chief clerk of the As
sembly and well known in political cir
cles. Mrs. Duckworth, whose end was
entirely unexpected, leaves a host of
friends to mourn her untimely taking off.
She will be buried this afternoon from
her sister's residence.

MIDNIGHT DUEL
IN IN ELLIS
STREET SALOON
"Jack" Wilson Got
His Man.
â â¦ â
WALTER CAVANAUGH KILLED
â Â»
HE WAS NOT QUICK ENOUGH
WITH HIS GUN.
â¦
Quarrel Over the Disreputable Sister
of the Dead Man, Who Was
the Mistress of His
Slayer.
Walter Cavanaugh. a young man well
known in the tenderloin, was shot and
killed by Jack Wilson shortly after mid
night this morning in the saloon of C. G.
Borgfeldt on the northwest corner of
Mason and Ellis streets. Three bullets en
tered his body and he died almost in
stantly. Wilson was arrested a few
minutes later by Officer Harrigan while
standing on the corner with the smoking
revolver still in his hand.
The quarrel which resulted in the kill
ing was by all accounts the second the
parties had during the day. Wilson has
been living with a sister of Cavanaugh at
5 Latham place, a small cul de sac off
Mason street in the rear of Borgfeidt's
saloon.
As nearly as can be ascertained, Cava
naugh had been living off the profits of
his sister's degradation. The relation
ship of Wilson with Miss Cavanaugh, it
is said, interfering as it did with the
money supply of Cavanaugh, gave rise
to flip Quarrel.
Cavanaugh in a drunken condition
called yesterday afternoon about 6 o'clock
at the residence of his sister and Wilson.
He had been there but a short time when
the neighbors heard two pistol shots and
saw the woman run out on the porch,
calling upon her brother to come out and
go away.
Mrs. M. A. Carson and her son. Thomas.
who reside at 3 Latham place, heard two
shots. Half an hour later they saw
Cavanaugh reel down the alley to the
street alone. Miss Laura Graham of 1 ;
Latham place also saw Cavanaugh take j
his departure. ..'â¢'. j
About 12:30 o'clock this morning, while
Borgfeldt was closing the door of his
grocery, he heard men's voices as if
raised in quarrel coming from the side
walk. He next heard the saloon doors
open and the men come in.
"Have you a gun?" Borgfeldt heard
pome one exclaim. The next Instant
shots rang out. The saloon man thinks
there were four.
Instead of entering the saloon, he ran
out to the sidewalk through the door of
the grocery, where he saw Wilson stand
ing, with a revolver in hand. Officer Har
rigan. who was on Ellis street and heard
the shots, came up in a moment and
placed Wilson under arrest. The pris
oner told the policeman that he shot in
self-defense.
Inside the saloon door, lying in a pool
of gore, with fragments of glass all about
him, lay the dead man. His features were
Indistinguishable.
A revolver belonging to the dead man
was lying beside him, having fallen from
his grasp as the bullets struck him. Three
chambers were empty, another contained
an exploded cartridge shell and the fourth
an unexploded bullet, bearing out the the
ory that Cavanaugh had fired one if not
the first shot in the quarrel.
The pistol Wilson had when arrested
contained three exploded cartridges.
Stonewall Bailey, a bartender, was with
Wilson when he killed Cavanaugh.
"We were standing at the corner of
Ellis and Mason streets," he remarked,
"engaged in conversation. I saw Cava
naugh approaching us and, knowing the
bitter feeling that existed between the
men, I advised Wilson to watch him
closely.
"Just then Cavanaugh, who was accom
panied by another man, named "Wallace, I
think, accosted Wilson, and, drawing a
revolver, asked him if he was heeled.
"Wilson replied that he did not want to
fcave any trouble and advised Cavanaugh
to put up his gun.
"Cavanaugh raised his revolver as if to
shoot, when his friend grabbed him and
attempted to take the weapon away from
him.
"Wilson at this juncture started to enter
the saloon on the corner and was quickly
followed by Cavanaugh. The latter still
â  held his gun In his hand and again level
ing it at Wilson fired.
"Wilson immediately drew hft revolver
and fired three shots at Cavanaugh, in
stantly killing him."
Wilson was arrested immediately after
the killing and taken to the City Prison.
lie gave his age as 35 years and his oc
-1 cupation as that of a bartender. He was
1 pale and excited and begged to be given
a drink of whisky.
Jack Wallace, who was with Cavanaugh
at the time of the shooting, disappeared
j immediately after.
| When questioned about the killing Wil
son declared that he did not want to dis
cuss the crime until he had consulted an
attorney.
"When the proper time comes." he re
marked, "I will show that I acted In self
| defense. If I had not shot Cavanaugh he
I would have sent me to the Morgue. It is
i ! true that I have been living with his sis
' ter, but she had nothing to do with the
t j shooting."
NYMPHIA AGAIN ATTACKED.
Five Directors Are to Be Arrested on
Warrants.
To-day another move will be made,
aimed to end the existence of the Hotel
Nymphia. Warrants will be sworn out
for five directors of the Twinkling Star
Improvement Company, which company
conducts the new enterprise. The parties
to be arrested are L. P. E. Frey. Emil
Kehrleln, Valentine H. Kehrlein, W. H.
Richards and S. P. Blumenberg. Attor
ney John J. Barrett, acting for the citi
zens of North Beach, will swear to the
complaints and will assist in the prosecu
tion. .â¢ , " :â  â â  '.
Attorney Barrett stated yesterday that
he Is taking this action because of a fear
that the recent decision handed down by
Judge Bahrs in Geary-street turntable
case might affect the legality of the ordi
nance passed a few days ago by the
Board of Supervisors condemning the Ho
tel Nvmphia as a public nuisance. That
something may be done immediately to
suppress the place he will test the valid
ity of its existence by the arrest of its
promoters. Attorney Joseph Dunn will
appear for the directors. The warrants
j will be sworn out in Judge Graham's
court.
CRUSHED HER HAND.
Miss Theresa Schoopkin Received Se
vere Injuries in a Printing House.
Miss Theresa Schoopkin, who resides
with her family at 317 Green street, met
with an accident yesterday which is like
ly to result in the loss of her right hand.
The young lady is employed by the Stu
parich Manufacturing Company at 141
Fremont street, and was engaged in ope
rating a press which prints labels on pa
per boxes. In some manner her fingers
became entangled in the large rollers and
before assistance could reach her the ma
chinery had frightfully lacerated her
hand. All possible attention was given
the unfortunate girl and she was hastily
taken to the Harbor Receiving Hospital,
where Dr. Thompson dressed the injured
member. He was obliged to' take nearly
fifty stitches in the flesh, and at present
he expresses his doubts whether the hand
can be saved. Miss Schoopkin is about 20
years of age.

MINNIE ADAMS
IS GUILTY OF
CHILD MURDER
The Degenerate Poi
soned Her Baby.
Mrs. Minnie Adams has been convicted
of the murder of her infant son. John
Richard Gray. That she will not walk to
the gallows is due alone to the sympathy
of the twelve good men and true whs ;
passed judgment upon her. They have ,
fixed the' penalty for the crime at im- ,
prisonment for life.
Saturday morning at 11 o'clock the fate
of the murderess was placed in the hands
of the Jury. After an hour's discussion of
the facts of the case a ballot was taken,
and it is said that nine condemned her for
the crime while three stood for her inno
cence. Another discussion followed, and
the second ballot found but one man,
Juror Mehrtens. favorable to the accused.
Then the work of, bringing this juror to
a belief in the prisoner's guilt began. At j
6 o'clock he had not wavered, and Judge
Lawlor ordered the jury locked up for the
night. Sunday morning came, and still
he voted for acquittal. The jury was sent
back for further deliberation, and again
at 1 o'clock was called into the court
room. Foreman Manning stated that the
lone juror simply sat mute and discussion
of the case had ended. Again the jury
was sent back, and at 6 o'clock the same
scene was enacted. . Again the jury went !
back, and at 3 o'clock Monday morning j
Judge Lawlor was informed that the jury j
wanted certain testimony read. Judge j
Lawlor hurried to the courtroom, and then
it was made known that Attorney Mack,
who represented the accused, had left the j
city leaving Judge A. D. Lemon to watch I
the interests of his client. The city was
scoured for Lemon without avail, and i
finally Attorney W. W. Davidson, whose
address was near the court, was appoint- >
ed by Judge Lawlor to represent the ac
cused. Mr. Davidson consented to repre
sent the murderess after the condition of
affairs had been explained to him. Mrs.
Adams also gave her consent to the sub
stitution of counsel, and the jury filed in. I
It was only upon one point that Mr. Mehr
tens hungâ the point as to whether Har- j
| rison Tyrrell, the young brother of the
' accused,, had testified as to there being :
I carbolic acid, or any. liquid, in the bottle
: he found, from which' the poison was sub- â¢
1 sequently poured down the baby's throat, i
The testimony showed that there was not j
! any liquid in the bottle when it was j
j found, and a further reading developed |
I the fact that after Mrs. Adams threw it
| out of the window, after the baby's death,
; there was a quantity of poison left there- |
1 in. This was all the evidence sought. The i
I reading occupied until 3:45 o'clock,, and j
j half an hour later the bell announced
that a verdict had been reached.
Mrs. Adams was brought into court, and
her mother, Mrs. Kelly, and her brother,
John Tyrrell, were seated near. Fore
man Manning rose in response to the
question of the clerk as to whether or not
a verdict had been reached and said:
"We, the jury, find the defendant, Minnie j
j Adams, guilty of murder in the first de
gree, and lix and assess her punishment _
at confinement in the State's prison for
life. So say we all." Then he continued: I
"We all regret the painful duty that has |
been thrust upon usâ the painful duty of
passing judgment upon this woman for a |
crime that necessitates such a severe sen
tence. But it was our duty and we have I
obeyed the law." Judge Lawlor bowed |
and the juror took his seat. â¢
In the meantime Mrs. Adams remained I
mute, not a shadow of emotion crossing j
I her face. Judge Lawlor set August 19 as
the date of sentence, and then informed i
the bailiff to take the jurors to an early j
breakfast and when all had been satisfied |
to procure conveyances and see them to I
their homes. The court further thanked I
the jurors for the attention they gave the
; case and for their thorough deliberation
WHY "BRICK"
McPHERSON IS
VERY ANGRY
Cannot Get His Bail
Reduced.
"Brick" McPherson and Thomas J.
Clisham appeared before Judge Treadwell
yesterday for their preliminary examina
tion on the felony charge of obtaining
money by false pretenses. Prosecuting
Attorney Carpenter asked for a continu
ance till to-morrow afternoon, as the po
lice desired more time to get their evi
dence.
Attorney Harvey S. Brown, who repre
sents McPherson, objected strongly to a
continuance and .wanted the case gone on
with at once. His client had been a long
time in jail and was entitled to an im
mediate hearing.
Attorney J. N. E. Wilson then took a
hand in the argument, and asked that
McPherson's bail be reduced to $1000. He
said the defendant could get bondsmen to
that amount.
The Judge refused to reduce the bail
and made an order continuing the cases
till to-morrow. McPherson was very an
gry when taken back to the City Prison,
and remarked: "I guess they have all gone
back on me. Why should all these dubs
stay outside while I am in? Wait awhile;
I will say a few things in a day or so.
Clisham's walking around, air it he?
It was reported yesterday that Clisham
will be a witness for the prosecution
when McPherson's case come , up for. pre
liminary hearing. He insists that he was
an innocent victim in the conspiracy, and
in consideration of his testimony against
"Brick" he will, it la said, be ultimately
discharged from custody. is still play-
Saloon-keener E. N. Smoot is still plaj -
ing peek-a-boo with the police. Attorney
Madden who represents htm. said yester
day that Smoot was keeping under cover
till he could get sufficient bonds and
would surrender himself by to-night at
the latest It was rumored yesterday that
he had disposed of his saloon business on
I arkin and McAllister streets and would
shake the dust of San Francisco from his
fret but this was denied by his friends.
Walter Berry, alias Emert, the other man
in the swindle, has not been heard of. The
police have been unable to find any trace
of him.

much to the now condemned murderess.
This through. Judge Lawlor left the
bench and with a deputy sheriff on either
side Mrs. Adams left the court. On reach
ing the corridor a realization of her posi
tion came over her for the first time and
she swooned away. Her brother hurried
into the courtroom and procured water,
and soon she was able to resume her jour
ney to her cell. * *

MISSING PROM THE PRESIDIO.
Rollo M. Moffatt Supposed to Have
Been Foully Dealt With.
j. F. Wassun. chief packer at the Pre
sidio, visited police headquarters last
night and invoked the aid of Chief Lees
in finding Rollo M. Moffatt, whom he
fears has been foully dealt with. Moffatt
arrived here about a week ago from
Alaska; and went to work at the Pre
sidio. Saturday afternoon he disappeared
and has not been seen since. He had over
$300 in his pockets, and was possessed of
considerable jewelry and , several gold
nuggets. It is feared that he has fallen
among, thieves and has perhaps been
murdered. He is described as being about
26 years of age, 5 feet 8 inches in height
and weighs about 140 pounds. He waa
dressed ln a dark suit of clothes.
